One of the most important decisions you will make is what size to make the virtual machine for SQL Server in Azure. Size is effectively a combination of CPU, memory, and I/O choices. You make these choices today in your data center when you deploy SQL Server to a virtual machine or bare metal. You select how many CPUs, CPU speed, RAM, size of disks, and disk speed. So you need to be able to do the same thing with Azure.

Azure Virtual Machine sizes are categorized by Family and are known with a letter designation (B, D, E, …). If you are just trying to get started with SQL Server on Azure Virtual Machine, my recommendation is to pick a reasonable size to test out SQL Server vs. cost. If you are choosing a size for production, you need to carefully review size options. As you can imagine, the higher the horsepower, the higher the cost.

As Azure data centers bring in new models of computers to support virtual machine and Azure resources, new sizes support different hardware generations. Therefore, you will see virtual machine sizes like Ev4 or Ev5. VM sizes are comprised of several options in the form of a series of letters and numbers that make up the characteristics of VM performance and resources. I call it the “alphabet of virtual machine choices.” Let me show you through an example. Using the portal screen I’ve shown above for Basics, you can scroll down to see the size chosen by default. I will then use the Select size option as seen in Figure 3-5 to bring up a list of choices.

Figure 3-5

Choosing a VM size

You should now be presented with a screen that gives you choices on VM sizes. These tables can look daunting, but it gives you a feel of why you would pick a specific size because it dictates the number of CPUs, RAM, disks, IOPS, etc., you get for your VM deployment. Figure 3-6 shows results after I have typed in the “Search by VM size…” the string v5 which shows me some of the latest hardware for VMs.

Figure 3-6

VM size choices for Azure

I scrolled down after using this search and pointed out the choice I’ll use for this VM deployment called E4bds_v5. Let me use this example to point out what this screen shows about my selection and what some of the letter options mean.

First, you can see at the top columns that show for your choice a Type and then the maximum resources you get for vCPU, RAM, Data Disks, Max IOPS, Local Storage, Premium Disk Support, and estimated cost. The Type of Memory Optimized refers to a category of VM Size families that have the best memory to vCPU ratio because the number of vCPUs can affect how much RAM you get. For SQL Server, RAM is an important resource so the more memory per vCPU can benefit SQL Server workloads. You can see all the VM types available at https://aka.ms/azurevmsizes.

I am going to choose E4bds_v5 because it has plenty of CPUs and RAM for my purposes and enables a few options I need which I will describe shortly. If you look at the VM size choice, here is what each option means:

  • E = E is the E-series family which is one of the recommended VM sizes for SQL production workloads. The E-series has some of the latest innovations, latest processors, and optimized workloads that need memory like SQL Server.

  • 4 = Number of vCPUs for this choice.

  • b = Choosing the b option gives you a higher I/O to core ratio in the form of IOPS. If you look at the VM choice E4ds_v5, you can see it has a max IOPS of 6400 where the b option bumps this to 12100 with only a slightly higher cost.

  • d = The VM supports local storage. Local storage is a fast, local SSD drive for the VM. This is a critical choice for SQL Server since you can place tempdb on this drive and not have to worry about I/O performance. The local SSD drive is also known as a “scratch” drive since if a failover occurs for the VM to another host, any data is lost on this drive, but for tempdb that doesn’t matter since we recreate it on SQL Server startup. The amount of local storage you get is defined by the number of vCPUs you choose.

  • s = Premium managed disks are supported. You will learn more about why premium managed disks are important for SQL Server in the section later in this chapter titled “Maximizing Storage Performance.”

  • v5 = This is the family version. Microsoft is always innovating on VM choices, so any new major innovation (usually a choice for the latest hardware) comes in the form of a new family version. At the time of the writing of this book, the v6 for the E-series was in preview. Read more at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/virtual-machines/esv6-edsv6-series.

Tip

If you are looking for VM choices that use AMD processors, look for the letter a in the VM size. For example, the equivalent AMD choice for my above selection is E4ads_v5. It is important to note that the b option for higher I/O to core ratio is not available today for AMD.

You can learn more about the latest Microsoft recommendations for VM sizes for SQL Server at https://aka.ms/sqlIaaSSizing.

Note

You can change the VM size after you have deployed it without having to delete and recreate it. This is called resizing a VM. There are limits here, and it will require some downtime, but you can read more about resizing VMs at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/virtual-machines/sizes/resize-vm.

Accounts, Port, and OS Licensing

To finish off the default screen to deploy SQL Server in a virtual machine, you must provide an administrator account and password. For Windows, this becomes the local admin account and for Linux a root user. For a SQL Server gallery image deployment on Windows, this local admin account is automatically added to the sysadmin role (just like you would add yourself during SQL Server setup). The password for the admin account can be between 12 and 123 characters and must be a strong password with at least 3 of the following characters: 1 lower case, 1 upper case, 1 number, and 1 special character.

The next choice is to decide whether any ports for the virtual machine will be opened and available for inbound traffic. By default, port 3389 which is used by the Remote Desktop Connection (RDP) protocol is selected (for Linux, this would be port 22 which is the default port for the ssh protocol). Using this option is the most flexible but not the most secure option. I will leave this option for now, but later in this chapter in the section called “Connecting to Your VM,” I will talk about using different security options.

The final choice involves licensing for Windows deployments. When you choose a SQL Server gallery image with Windows, your license will be a pay as you go subscription for both SQL Server and Windows. This basically means you pay each month for a fixed cost of using Windows and SQL Server. You also pay cost for resource usage based on CPU usage and storage. If you own existing licenses for Windows Server, you can apply those licenses to your Azure Virtual Machine cost. You can read more about using Azure Hybrid Benefit for Windows Server at https://aka.ms/azurehybridbenefit.

Figure 3-7 shows the rest of the portal screen to fill out these options.

Figure 3-7

Choosing account, port, and license

At this point in the deployment process, you can click Review+Create and deploy your VM with other default options. However, let’s look at some of the other sections and why you might want to use them, especially the SQL Server Settings.

Making Configuration Choices As Part of Deploy

Let us look at the other options you can select as part of deploying SQL Server for Azure Virtual Machine.

OS Disks

Select Next: Disks > to see the options as seen in Figure 3-8.

Figure 3-8

Disk options for Azure Virtual Machine during deployment

The disk options presented here are for encryption at the host level and options for the disk supporting the operating system and other system files. While for all SQL Server deployments I recommend you choose the default options provided here, including premium managed disks, it is worth investigating the option called Encryption at Host, which will encrypt all disks at rest on the host. I will talk more about different types of managed disks you can use with SQL Server for Azure Virtual Machine in the section called “Maximizing Storage Performance.” I will recommend you take advantage of managed disks with SQL Server for the simple advantage of fault tolerance, but you may want to read more about unmanaged and ephemeral disks. You can read more about managed disks at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/virtual-machines/managed-disks-overview.

Note

If you install just an operating system image from the marketplace (e.g., Windows Server), you would get options here to add data disks. For SQL Server marketplace images, you will be able to add data disks in the configuration section below called “SQL Server Settings” or after you have deployed.

Networking

Now click Next: Networking >. Here, you are going to be presented with a set of choices to configure various aspects of networking for your virtual machine as seen in Figure 3-9.

Figure 3-9

Networking options during deploy for Azure Virtual Machine

The two options you may want to consider changing are the virtual network and subnet if this virtual machine needs to be included in an existing Azure virtual network. Notice in this example, a new virtual network will be created, including a name that contains my new VM. If you would have selected an existing resource group on the first screen to deploy, the virtual network associated with that resource group (if one exists) would be selected. I will discuss more about networking options for Azure Virtual Machine in the section later in this chapter called “Networking.”

The only other option I want to call out on this screen that I believe applies to your SQL Server on Azure Virtual Machine deployments is accelerated networking. Accelerated networking can be extremely beneficial for SQL Server deployments where client applications must communicate across a virtual network to SQL Server. Therefore, you may want to choose a VM size (or a VM size with the required number of CPUs) that supports accelerated networking. If you plan to just deploy SQL Server in a virtual machine and do everything inside the virtual machine, you do not need to worry about this option. You can read more about the benefits of accelerated networking at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/virtual-network/accelerated-networking-overview.

Note

You can later change the size of the virtual machine and then enable accelerated networking using PowerShell. You can read more about how to do this at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/virtual-network/create-vm-accelerated-networking-powershell.

There is one other option I’ve not shown here called Load Balancing options. While this option may be helpful for some application scenarios, for SQL Server a load balancer is not needed even for Always On Availability Groups (AG). I’ll discuss more about how to set up connectivity for an AG in the section titled “HADR” later in this chapter.

Management

Select Next: Management >. Figure 3-10 shows options that may be interesting to your deployment and use of SQL Server with Azure Virtual Machine.

Figure 3-10

Management options during deploy for Azure Virtual Machine

I highly recommend you consider Microsoft Defender for Cloud. My subscription has Defender enabled, so it is automatically enabled for my VM. Microsoft Defender for Cloud provides security capabilities for your virtual machine as well as SQL-specific features. Microsoft Defender for Cloud provides advanced threat protection and vulnerability assessments specific to potential security issues for a virtual machine in the cloud. In addition, Microsoft Defender for Cloud provides capabilities specific to SQL Server on Azure Virtual Machine. I’ll discuss more about how to configure this and its benefits in the section titled “Configuring an Azure Virtual Machine” later in this chapter.

Tip

At the time of the writing of this book, Microsoft has released a Copilot for Security in Defender for Cloud. Give it a try when you enable Defender at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/defender-for-cloud/copilot-security-in-defender-for-cloud.

System-assigned managed identity is an interesting option allowing you to create an identity in Microsoft Entra to use for authentication without having to put credentials in your code or application. One nice example to use this capability with Azure Virtual Machine is to connect to an Azure SQL Database from a virtual machine using Microsoft without prompting for any passwords. You can read more about managed identities at https://learn.microsoft.com/entra/identity/managed-identities-azure-resources/overview.

Enable auto-shutdown provides an option where the virtual machine will be shut down daily on the time of your choosing. Why would you ever select this for a SQL Server deployment? The primary reason is that you have a SQL Server deployment, and you have some downtime you can afford and want to save money. When a virtual machine is shut down, you only pay for your licensing and storage costs. I will talk more about stopping and starting virtual machines in the section titled “Configuration” later in this chapter.

Guest OS updates provides an option to have critical updates automatically applied to the OS inside the VM. There is a new option called Azure Update Manager you should consider because it allows you to apply even noncritical updates for both Windows Server and SQL Server. I’ll describe this capability more in the section titled “Configuration” later in this chapter.

Monitoring

Figure 3-11 shows options for monitoring.

Figure 3-11

Monitoring options during deploy for Azure Virtual Machine

Boot diagnostics are very helpful to see the serial output screen of your virtual machine on the host. This is like seeing the boot screen using a tool like Hyper-V Manager. You can read more about boot diagnostics at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/virtual-machines/boot-diagnostics.

I personally recommend you select OS guest diagnostics. This will allow you to see performance information from the guest OS in systems like Azure Monitor and even get alerts. I will discuss how to use this in the section titled “Performance Monitoring” later in this chapter.

If you deploy many Azure Virtual Machines, you should consider enabling virtual machine insights. This provides methods to monitor key information about the health and performance of your virtual machines at scale. Learn more at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-monitor/vm/vminsights-overview.

Advanced

With these options selected, click Next: Advanced >. As the name implies, these are advanced options that may interest you as seen in Figure 3-12.

Figure 3-12

Advanced options for Azure VM

Extensions are applications that run inside the VM that can provide post-deployment and automation tasks. The SQL Server gallery images have an extension used to configure SQL Server automatically if you use a gallery image as I’m doing here. There could be other extensions you want to explore at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/virtual-machines/extensions/features-windows.

There are other advanced options as you can see in Figure 3-13.

Figure 3-13

Other Advanced options for Azure VM

Host group defines an Azure Dedicated Host used for your virtual machine. The major benefit of an Azure Dedicated Host is a Dedicated Physical Server: the server is exclusively used by your organization, ensuring that no other customers share the same hardware. Capacity reservations allow you to reserve compute capacity in a specific Azure region or Availability Zone without a long-term commitment. I will discuss more about these topics in a later section of this chapter titled “Reserved Instances, Dedicated Hosts, and Capacity Reservations.”

Proximity placement groups are an interesting concept in Azure that allows you to request multiple Azure resources be located as close as possible within an Azure data center to provide the lowest network latency possible. This may be an option to consider if the application to connect to SQL Server in Azure Virtual Machine will be hosted in Azure.

Note

I would not recommend using proximity groups for a HA solution for SQL Server in Azure Virtual Machines such as Availability Groups. In this scenario, you will use a concept called Availability Sets or Availability Zones. I will discuss these concepts later in the chapter in a section titled “HADR.”

Click Next: SQL Server Settings > to see the next set of options.

SQL Server Settings

Up to this point, all the configuration choices are independent of SQL Server. This screen provides configuration choices for deployment specific to SQL Server. If you know the SQL Server setup experience, we have provided in the latest releases the ability to “configure when you install” options. This means we have included options you normally configure after installation into the process itself. We are now trying to provide this same experience in Azure Virtual Machine but also include options that allow an optimal configuration for Azure.

Let us look at these choices in more detail. Figure 3-14 shows choices for networking, security, and storage.

Figure 3-14

SQL Server Settings for network, security, and storage

Note

As of the time of writing this book, SQL Server Linux images do not support SQL Server Settings during deployment.

Networking provides a choice on how to expose the SQL Server instance default TCP port 1433. Think of this choice like using a firewall. Private means any Azure source within the virtual network of the VM can access this port. This is the default and one I recommend you use. Local means the only access is allowed inside the VM. Public means the TPC port is exposed on the Internet. As tempting as it may be to use the public option so you can connect with a tool like SSMS from your laptop to this deployed VM, I do not recommend using this option.

Note

The first time I deployed a SQL Server in Azure VM using the public option, I immediately got attacked from outside intruders trying to log in using the sa account guessing password. I found this out when I saw my ERRORLOG flooded with login failed messages. This occurred almost immediately after deploying the VM.

The choice for SQL Authentication is identical to enabled mixed mode security for SQL Server. Even though the sa login is disabled by default if you choose to enable this option, you will be prompted for a SQL login that will be granted SQL Server sysadmin rights for your deployment.

Azure Key Vault integration is a choice you might want to enable, but do not worry, you can enable this post deployment. Azure Key Vault integration may be helpful to ease your use of Azure Key Value for scenarios like Transparent Data Encryption (TDE). You can read more about Azure Key Vault integration with SQL Server in Azure Virtual Machine at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/virtual-machines/windows/azure-key-vault-integration-configure.

Storage configuration is perhaps one of the most important choices you will make for SQL Server in Azure Virtual Machine especially for performance. Let’s look at what choices you can make and how we provide a guided experience to configuration storage for your databases.

Note

There is a new experience in preview to use Azure Premium SSD v2 Storage. I’ll discuss more about this option in the section titled “Maximizing Storage Performance” later in this chapter.

If you click Change configuration, you will see options such as seen in Figure 3-15.

Figure 3-15

Configuring storage for SQL Server in Azure Virtual Machine

This screen is divided into three sections: (1) database file storage, (2) transaction file storage, and (3) tempdb storage.

For database and transaction files, you are provided options for location, managed disk type, disk size type, and number of disks. For databases, you also have the option to have system databases (master, model, and msdb) moved off the OS drive and to the drive where database files are placed. Unless you are just doing some basic testing, I always recommend the following:

  • Choose to have the data and log on separate drives.

  • Use the Premium SSD disk type.

  • Choose a disk size type that is large enough to handle the necessary size, IOPS, and throughput for the I/O SQL Server needs for your application.

Premium SSD disk types come in various sizes with a designation of P<n> from sizes 4GB (P1) to 32TB (P80). The performance in terms of IOPS and throughput increases with larger sizes. One problem you may face is trying to align the IOPS and throughput you need with your minimum size requirement. This may require you to overprovision the size (or multiple disks) to get the performance you need (as I mentioned earlier, there is a new disk type called Premium SSD v2 in preview that can help here; you will learn about this in the section titled “Maximizing Storage Performance” later in the chapter). You can read more about the various Premium SSD disk types at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/virtual-machines/disks-types#premium-ssds.

For tempdb, notice how the recommendation is to place it on the local SSD drive and recommend number of files and sizes for both data and log, very much like SQL Server setup does today.

Notice the warning at the bottom of the screen. It says the configured disks have a performance vector that is greater than what the VM size supports. This is an important warning because it means the VM size you have selected doesn’t support enough IOPS and/or throughput even though you selected storage that meets your requirements. This is a hard problem to detect when it occurs, so getting the VM size right now to meet your storage performance requirements is important. You will need to go back to the Basics screen first and change the VM size and then come back to this screen to make sure the warning is gone.

Below storage configuration is the ability to change SQL Server instance settings as seen in Figure 3-16.

Figure 3-16

Choosing SQL Server instance settings

Click Change SQL instance settings and you will get a screen like Figure 3-17.

Figure 3-17

Additional options for SQL Server Settings

You can see on this screen various options you can choose during SQL Server setup (remember that when you deploy SQL Server in Azure VM using the portal, we automatically run setup for you to install everything!), such as server collation, MAXDOP, max and min memory, and optimize for ad hoc workload settings. In addition, we provide you two popular options for performance optimization: lock pages in memory and instant file initialization. You can configure all of these after you deploy, but having the opportunity to do this now just saves you time and effort.

After you select OK, you have other choices you can make as seen in Figure 3-18.

Figure 3-18

Additional SQL Server Settings

SQL Server License allows you to testify that you have an existing SQL Server license that allows you to apply it for Azure Hybrid Benefit (AHB) like the choice for Windows. This can be a significant cost savings to you when using SQL Server for Azure Virtual Machine.

Note

If you do not see this option available, it is because you picked a SQL Server image choice where this option cannot be or is already selected. For example, images that start with the title BYOL (this means Bring Your Own License) already imply you are just using our own license. Developer Edition is free, so no license applies.

We also announced in 2019 new licensing benefits for HA and DR scenarios when using Azure Virtual Machine. You can read more at https://cloudblogs.microsoft.com/sqlserver/2019/10/30/new-high-availability-and-disaster-recovery-benefits-for-sql-server/.

Automated patching provides specific configurations on when to deploy important and critical updates for Windows and SQL Server. Other updates for Windows and SQL Server will depend on how you configure Windows Update inside the VM. As I mentioned earlier, there is a new option called Azure Update Manager you should consider because it allows you to apply even noncritical updates for both Windows Server and SQL Server. I’ll describe this capability more in the section titled “Configuration” later in this chapter.

Automated backup uses the managed backups to Azure capability that was shipped as part of SQL Server 2016. Your needs may vary, but many users might find this option very useful to provide a simple automated backup solution for SQL Server. When you enable this option, you have several choices to configure how backups are executed.

Note

Automated patching and automated backup options may not show up as enabled immediately after deployment. The SQL IaaS extension runs in the background after deployment.

R Services (Advanced Analytics) is an option to install the SQL Server Machine Learning services feature as part of deployment. I will describe in the next section called “Deploy!” what exactly is installed with the SQL Server gallery image.

Click Next: Tags > to see the last option.

Tags

The last option before you deploy your virtual machine is to potentially use a tag as seen in Figure 3-19. Tags are a concept supported by the Azure ecosystem to assign a string value to a resource in Azure, like a virtual machine, to organize your resources.

Figure 3-19

Assigning tags to an Azure resource

Click Next: Review + create > to validate and deploy the virtual machine.

Deploy!

The portal will take all your options, perform validation steps, and then present you the ability to create the virtual machine. Figure 3-20 shows some interesting information on the final validation screen before you click Create.

Figure 3-20

Validation before creating the virtual machine

At the top of this screen, you can see the size of the VM you chose plus estimated costs per hour. Also, notice Terms of use and Privacy policy. The End User Licensing Agreement (EULA) that comes with SQL Server still applies to Azure Virtual Machine since this is a fully licensed SQL Server. However, since you are deploying the virtual machine in Azure, there are terms and privacy policies you should review. You can read more about Azure terms of use at https://azure.microsoft.com/support/legal. Privacy is a very important topic, and since Microsoft is hosting your virtual machine in the cloud, you need to understand all the details of what information Microsoft collects. Read more at https://privacy.microsoft.com/privacystatement. Your usage of a gallery image for the operating system and/or SQL Server also has terms called Azure Marketplace Terms. You can read more at https://azure.microsoft.com/support/legal/marketplace-terms/.

You will also notice on this screen a warning about allowing the RDP port to be exposed to the Internet. You will learn more about how to control access and limit any issues in the section below titled “Connecting to Your VM.” If you scroll down on this screen, you will see all the details of the options you chose to deploy the virtual machine. Notice also at the bottom of the screen the option to Download a template for automation. I will discuss using an option for automation with a template in a section later in this chapter called “Using a CLI with ARM Templates, Bison, and Terraform.”

Click the Create button when you are ready to launch! The deployment of the virtual machine is asynchronous, so you can even exit the portal, and the deployment is done in the background. However, if you leave the portal screen open, you can track progress live. Within seconds of clicking create, my screen looks like Figure 3-21 to track the progress of the deployment.

Figure 3-21

Azure Virtual Machine deployment in progress

Not only does the main screen refresh as the virtual machine is being created, but you can click the Notifications icon on the title bar of the portal to also track progress. In my example, in about ten minutes my deployment was complete. Figure 3-22 shows all the details, including status from the Notifications icon.

Figure 3-22

Deployment complete for SQL Server in Azure Virtual Machine

You will notice for Deployment details that it is not just a virtual machine deployed but many different resources, including network interfaces, disks, storage accounts, virtual networks, and security groups. If you want more details about the deployment, you can click More events in the Activity Log.

If you click Go to resource, you will be presented with the overview screen for the virtual machine. There is another view of this virtual machine from a SQL Server perspective I’ll cover later. Let’s take a tour on how to navigate the overview screen for an Azure Virtual Machine.

Navigating an Azure Virtual Machine in the Portal

You will find yourself often using several aspects to the overview screen for Azure Virtual Machine and all Azure resources in this book. Let us examine the main areas of the overview screen as seen in Figure 3-23.

Figure 3-23

The overview screen for Azure Virtual Machine

  1. Service Menu

    Each Azure resource provides a series of options to manage the resource. The very top of the Service Menu is the same for all resources. You will want to use the Overview option to “find your way back” if you are deep into using blades or sections for details on the virtual machine. This is super important as I often have hit the X at the top right of a screen and completely lost the context of my VM. By clicking Overview on the Service Menu, I can keep that context.

    Under Overview, we will see options to look for Activity Log entries specific to this VM, the ability to control access outside the VM using RBAC, set new tags, or diagnose problems. Underneath this is a series of category options that you can expand such as Connect to connect to the VM, Network to configure network options, or Settings to configure specific aspects of the VM. There are other options as well you can explore. I will not go over each option here but will use several of these options throughout the rest of the chapter.

     

  2. Command Bar

    These are buttons that allow you to operate the virtual machine. Every Azure resource has buttons unique to the resource. The most common buttons you will use are Connect, Start, Restart, and Stop. I’ll cover more the significance of stopping the VM using this option in the section later in this chapter titled “Stopping vs. Deallocating.”

     

  3. Essentials

    Technically, this is part of the Working Pane. This area displays key information about your virtual machine, including resource group, region, status, OS version, and VM size, but also lets you navigate to certain aspects of the virtual machine such as virtual network.

     

  4. Working Pane

    The Working Pane includes sections to see detailed properties of the VM, monitoring with health events, alerts, and performance metrics, enabling capabilities like Windows Admin Center, recommendations, and training tutorials.

     

Explore more about how to navigate around the portal at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-portal/azure-portal-overview#getting-around-the-portal.

Connecting to Your VM

Now that you have deployed, one of the first things you will want to do is connect to the VM. For a Windows VM, the most popular way to connect and use the VM is with the Remote Desktop Protocol (RDP). You saw in the VM deployment example that I chose the option to open the RDP port (3389) to the Internet.

To use RDP, click the Connect button in the Command Bar and choose Connect. You see the options to connect as in Figure 3-24.

Figure 3-24

Connecting with RDP to Azure Virtual Machine

You can connect via RDP by selecting Download RDP file. The RDP file will be copied to your Downloads folder on your client machine. The public IP address for the VM is also posted here if you just want to take any valid RDP client and connect directly to the VM. Once you connect, just like any VM or Windows computer you will be prompted to enter your credentials (which you supplied as part of deployment), which by default is a member of the Administrators group.

Note

If you notice under Download RDP file, there are options to help you troubleshoot any RDP connectivity issues which I highly recommend you look at, should you encounter any issues.

If you use Select, you can also see on this screen a recommendation to enable just-in-time access to the VM to improve security. Just-in-time access is a way to gain RDP access to the VM “on-demand” so that the RDP port is not opened to the Internet when you are not using the VM. This is a way to allow you to connect to the VM from any client yet limit the exposure of the RDP port to everyone. You can learn more about just-in-time access at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/defender-for-cloud/just-in-time-access-usage.

The other method you can see on this screen is to use a more secure method called Azure Bastion, which you can learn more about at https://azure.microsoft.com/services/azure-bastion/. I’ve run into customers where their company blocks them from using RDP on their laptop, so this browser-based method is an excellent, more secure option to use to connect to the VM.

You may also connect with standard SQL tools like SSMS from another Azure VM that you have deployed in the same Azure virtual network. I often will deploy another “client” Azure Virtual Machine in the same virtual network, so I can privately connect using SQL tools to my SQL Server deployment.

Exploring the SQL Server Installation

I thought you might find it interesting to know exactly what we install and how we configure the SQL Server instance when you deploy with SQL Server gallery images.

What Is Installed

A SQL Server gallery image installs the entire database engine, SQL Server Analysis Services, SQL Server Integration Services (SSIS), MDS, and DQS. In addition, we install the following engine features:

  • SQL Server Agent

  • SQL Server Replication

  • Full-text search

We also install client, tools, SDK, and SQL Server Management Studio. We do not install Polybase by default. As I have described earlier in this chapter, you can install R Services as part of the deployment based on an option you choose.

Note

Your license includes the ability to deploy SQL Server Reporting Services, but like SQL Server, we don’t install that. You can deploy this yourself using https://learn.microsoft.com/sql/reporting-services/install-windows/install-reporting-services. In addition, customers with SA agreements using Enterprise Edition have license rights to install Power BI Report Server (https://www.microsoft.com/power-platform/products/power-bi/report-server).

For a SQL Server gallery image, we copy the SQL Server media, so you can install or remove any features you want. You can find all the setup files in the C:\SQLServerFull folder in the VM. Figure 3-25 shows an example of what features are installed by default using a SQL Server 2022 gallery image.

Figure 3-25

SQL Server features installed by default

What Is Configured

The following is a list of configuration choices we make as part of a SQL Server gallery image installation:

  • SQL Server, SQL Server Agent, full-text, and SSIS services are set to automatic and are running after deployment. Service SIDs are used for all services.

  • The VM admin account you specify during deployment becomes a SQL sysadmin.

  • The filestream feature is disabled by default (but can be enabled and supported).

  • Always On Availability Groups is disabled by default (but can be enabled and supported).

  • The TCP/IP protocol is enabled for SQL Server (even for Developer Edition).

  • We typically have a recent Cumulative Update (and/or security update) installed, but it may not be the exact latest Cumulative Update. You can read more about how we keep our images up to date at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/virtual-machines/windows/sql-server-on-azure-vm-iaas-what-is-overview?view=azuresql#lifecycle.

  • CEIP is enabled by default, but you can disable it. Read more at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/virtual-machines/windows/sql-server-on-azure-vm-iaas-what-is-overview?view=azuresql#customer-experience-improvement-program-ceip.

  • Microsoft SQL Server VSS Writer is installed by default.

Deploy on Your Own

Today when I deploy a SQL Server instance in a Hyper-V virtual machine (often just on my laptop), I go through the process of choosing certain options for the VM (like number of logical CPUs, memory, location of disks) and then install an operating system like Windows Server or Linux (typically from an .ISO file). When this is done, I usually connect to the VM with Remote Desktop (Windows) or ssh (Linux). For Windows, I will download the installation media for SQL Server to my local drive and then “copy and paste” it into a folder in the VM. I then run setup and am off and running to deploy SQL Server. For Linux, I just make sure my VM is connected to the Internet and run the SQL Server on Linux installation which downloads packages as part of the installation process.

This same process is almost identical to the “deploy on your own” option for SQL Server on Azure Virtual Machine, with one exception. You will configure the VM and deploy an operating system by using one of the gallery images just for the operating system. You can use the portal, az CLI, or PowerShell to choose your VM size and operating system, very similar to using a SQL Server gallery image except all the options for SQL Server will not be part of the process.

This technique is one I sometimes use because I have complete control of how and what is installed with SQL Server vs. what Microsoft chooses from a gallery image. If you use this method, there is one downside. You do not immediately get to take advantage of the options you get with a SQL gallery image, such as automated backups and security updates. Fortunately, there is a solution to use this customized method for deployment but take advantage of automated features and licensing options. This solution is called the SQL Server IaaS Agent Extension (which is installed by default when using a gallery image). I will discuss more about this capability in the section titled “SQL Server IaaS Agent Extension” later in the chapter.

Using a CLI with ARM Templates, Bison, and Terraform

Azure provides other ways to deploy SQL Server on Azure Virtual Machine besides the Azure portal using a command-line interface (CLI) with the az sql vm or az vm CLI. There are also PowerShell commands New-AzVM and New-AzSqlVM. The problem is that all of these methods don’t support a way to configure optimal storage settings as I have described earlier with the portal. You would have to manually configure this after you deployed.

Azure provides a mechanism for any Azure resource to automate the use of Azure CLI tools with a concept called an ARM template. An ARM template is a JSON file that describes in a declarative fashion the configuration and infrastructure of a deployment.

While you can try to learn how to build templates from scratch, I recommend you get an example. You can download and edit an example of an Azure template to create a new SQL Server in Azure Virtual Machine with configurable storage settings at https://learn.microsoft.com/samples/azure/azure-quickstart-templates/sql-vm-new-storage. Azure templates not only configure all the properties to create the virtual machine but support parameters, so you can use a template and then supply the parameters you want to customize the deployment at runtime. The link for this template also includes a file to support deploying a SQL Server VM on Azure with Bison.

Terraform can also be used to deploy SQL Server on Azure VM. You can read more at https://registry.terraform.io/providers/hashicorp/azurerm/latest/docs/resources/mssql_virtual_machine.

Reserved Instances, Dedicated Hosts, and Capacity Reservations

To save money on Azure Virtual Machines, you can prepay for a period (one to three years) and then save on the compute costs of the Azure Virtual Machine as you deploy. This option is called Azure Reserved VM Instances. If you plan to deploy many SQL Server Azure Virtual Machines, you probably should investigate this option. Learn more about Reserved Instances at https://azure.microsoft.com/pricing/reserved-vm-instances/.

Capacity Reservations allow you to reserve compute capacity in an Azure region or Availability Zone for any duration of time. This is particularly useful for ensuring that you have the necessary resources available for critical workloads, even during peak demand periods. Capacity Reservations are more flexible than Reserved Instances because there is no major long-term commitment. They are available almost immediately, and you have full control how you target your reservation for critical workloads. However, Reserved Instances, because of their long-term commitment, are less expensive than Capacity Reservations. You can learn more about Capacity Reservations at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/virtual-machines/capacity-reservation-overview.

You might remember there was an option during the deployment example in the portal called a dedicated host. An Azure Dedicated Host allows you to reserve physical servers dedicated to you and your organization. While normal virtual machines are dedicated to your deployment, you are typically sharing the underlying hosts with other users. A dedicated host may give you the option you need for specific compliance requirements. In addition, you have more control over maintenance events of the infrastructure hosting your VM deployment. You can read more about dedicated hosts at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/virtual-machines/dedicated-hosts. The largest virtual machine in this group is the Standard_E104ids_v5 that offers 104 vCores and 672 GiBs of memory. This virtual machine is notable because it’s isolated which means it’s guaranteed to be the only virtual machine running on the host and therefore is isolated from other customer workloads.

Migration to Azure

You might be considering migration of an existing SQL Server installation to Azure Virtual Machine. Here are the options that might help your migration plans.

Azure Arc Migration Assessment

I briefly mentioned in Chapter 1 the concept of Azure Arc to connect a SQL Server to the cloud. While I talk more about Azure Arc in Chapter 10 of the book, I’ll mention here there is an innovation (in preview at the time of the writing of this book) to help you assess your current SQL Server deployment to see if it is compatible to migration to the cloud, including what are the right deployment decisions and resource choices. Learn more about how to use this capability at https://learn.microsoft.com/sql/sql-server/azure-arc/migration-assessment.

Restoring a Database

Since SQL Server in Azure Virtual Machine is a full SQL Server engine, one simple way to migrate an existing SQL Server is to just restore a backup of existing databases.

Since the target SQL Server is in the Azure infrastructure, you have a few ways to do this:

  • Copy the backup file with “copy and paste” over RDP. RDP clients allow you to copy and paste files, and I have done this a bunch of times for Azure VM. Of course, this only makes sense for small backup files (although I have done this with backup files even 1GB in size).

  • Back up the database to Azure Storage using the backup to URL functionality within SQL Server. Then in the Azure VM, restore the backup from the same Azure Storage account. You can read more about SQL Server backup to URL at https://learn.microsoft.com/sql/relational-databases/backup-restore/sql-server-backup-to-url.

  • Use Azure Files. Think of this like creating your own file share in the cloud. You can use az CLI or tools to copy your backup file to the Azure file share. Then you can mount the Azure file share within the Azure VM (i.e., it will look like a network share in the VM). Read more about how to do this at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/storage/files/storage-files-introduction. I have used this method several times for both Azure VMs for Windows and Linux.

  • For large backup files, consider the Azure Import/Export service. You literally will securely ship a hard drive to Microsoft (we can supply you the method with an Azure Data Box Disk), and we will import this to Azure Storage or Azure Files. You can read more about this at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/import-export/storage-import-export-service.

Azure Migrate and Azure Database Migration Service

Let’s say you wanted a more online migration experience to Azure Virtual Machine. You have that option using the Azure Database Migration Service (DMS). DMS is a managed service in Azure that orchestrates a migration to Azure SQL, including Azure Virtual Machine. One of the options for this migration is to do an online migration for SQL Server to Azure VM. DMS will use log shipping technology to take a series of continuous database full and transaction log backups and restore them to an already deployed SQL Server on Azure VM. See an example of how to use this service at https://learn.microsoft.com/data-migration/sql-server/virtual-machines/database-migration-service?tabs=online-with-extension.

If you want to assess and migrate SQL Server at scale, consider using Azure Migrate. Azure Migrate is an Azure service and set of client software agents to assess and migrate SQL Server running in virtual machines and bare-metal servers to Azure. Azure Migrate can coordinate with Azure Database Migration Service to help you perform offline and/or online migrations. Get started at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/migrate/how-to-create-azure-sql-assessment.

Using Azure Migrate Server Migration

What if you would like to migrate the installation of an entire physical server or virtual machine to Azure instead of just your data? Azure supports this concept with a service called Azure Migrate Server Migration. This is a true lift and shift operation. Think of this like taking a snapshot of your machine or VM and creating an entire VM from the snapshot. Then you configure and optimize after the fact. You can read more about Server Migration at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/migrate/migrate-services-overview#azure-migrate-server-migration-tool. A great example of how this works for VMware installation can be seen at https://aka.ms/mechanicsazuremigrate with legendary Windows guru Jeff Woolsey.

Deploying SQL Server on Linux with Azure Virtual Machine

Up to this point, my examples and discussion have centered around deploying SQL Server in Azure Virtual Machines based on Windows Server.

The options and deployment process are very similar for SQL Server on Linux with some notable differences:

  • The gallery images are offered for Linux distributions Ubuntu, Red Hat Enterprise Server, and SUSE. You pay for licenses if the Linux distribution requires it (Ubuntu is a free license).

  • We do not support SQL Server Settings during deployment. The SQL Server IaaS Agent extension is supported on Ubuntu distributions but provides limited support for licensing. Learn more at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/virtual-machines/linux/sql-server-iaas-agent-extension-linux.

After using a SQL Linux gallery image, you will find the mssql-server, mssql-tools, and mssql-ha packages have been installed.

SQL Agent is not enabled, but you can do this yourself. You can enable SQL Server Agent on Linux with the mssql-conf script which you can read more about at https://learn.microsoft.com/sql/linux/sql-server-linux-setup-sql-agent.

Post deployment, you can use documented methods to install other SQL Server Linux packages. For example, you can read https://learn.microsoft.com/sql/linux/sql-server-linux-setup-machine-learning to learn how to install SQL Server Machine Learning Services for Linux.

Deploying SQL Server Containers

There is no special gallery image or process to deploy SQL Server containers in Azure Virtual Machine. You will use the same process you use today to deploy a SQL Server container in a virtual machine. You can read more about this process at https://aka.ms/sqlcontainers.

Azure Kubernetes Service (AKS) provides a method to deploy containers at scale using the power of Kubernetes. You can see a tutorial of how to do this at https://learn.microsoft.com/sql/linux/quickstart-sql-server-containers-azure.

SQL Server IaaS Agent Extension

You have seen examples in this chapter of special options and advantages of deploying a SQL Server in Azure Virtual Machine with a gallery image, including licensing, configuration, and automation. This is all made possible through the SQL Server IaaS Agent Extension using the SQL Virtual Machine Resource Provider. The resource provider is software running in Azure that enables specific SQL capabilities integrated with Azure Resource Manager. The extension is a set of agents running in your virtual machine that use the resource provider.

If you deploy using a SQL Server gallery image, you just take advantage of what the resource provider does, and no action is required from you. However, if you deploy on your own and want these advantages, including licensing, you will need to register your VM as a SQL Azure VM.

First, you need to ensure your Azure subscription has the Microsoft.SqlVirtualMachine resource provider registered. You can do this from the Azure Portal as part of your Azure subscription. Or you can use the following PowerShell command: 

Register-AzureRmResourceProvider -ProviderNamespace Microsoft.SqlVirtualMachine

Next, you need to register your virtual machine with the resource provider. Registering your VM installs the SQL Server IaaS Agent Extension. You can read about all of the steps at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/virtual-machines/windows/sql-agent-extension-manually-register-single-vm.

Once your VM is registered or if you used a SQL gallery image, your VM is now considered both an Azure Virtual Machine resource and a SQL virtual machine. This means your VM will show up when searching for Azure SQL resources. Additionally, the portal will show additional properties as seen in Figure 3-26 based on the deployment I made earlier in this chapter.

Figure 3-26

A registered SQL Azure Virtual Machine

Note

One easy way to get to this overview page is to go to the Command Menu of your virtual machine and select SQL Server Configuration under Settings. Then click SQL Server Configuration.

You can see this screen looks like an Azure Virtual Machine but information and options specific to SQL Server.

You can see the Essentials pane has information about the VM but also SQL Server, including version, edition, license type, and OS details. Underneath the Working Pane is a graph showing storage for database and log files and features you can enable for SQL Server in Azure VM. The Command Bar has an option to delete (unregister) the SQL Server Azure Virtual Machine and delete the underlying VM.

The Service Menu (expanded in Figure 3-26) has options specific to SQL Server running on Azure Virtual Machine, including the following two main categories. I think you will find several of these options extremely valuable in helping you manage SQL Server on Azure Virtual Machines.

Settings

Under Settings, you have the following options.

Configure

This option allows you to specify the license for SQL (Pay As You Go, Azure Hybrid Benefit, or DR).

I’ve described Azure Hybrid Benefit earlier in this chapter. Pay As You Go is the default billing method to be billed for the SQL License per month. DR is a new option to offer a free SQL Server license if the SQL Server in the VM is used only for disaster recovery purposes.

In addition, you can specify which edition of SQL Server you are using for the VM.

Storage

This option is worth looking at as it provides analysis, metrics, assessments, and configuration of storage as it relates to SQL Server for the VM as seen in Figure 3-27.

Figure 3-27

Storage assistance for SQL Server in Azure VM

Updates

This is another nice option to manage not just security updates but other updates for Windows and Cumulative Updates for SQL Server. You can even manage and schedule updates at scale. This capability is based on the Azure Update Manager. Learn more at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/virtual-machines/azure-update-manager-sql-vm.

Backups

This feature allows you to enable and disable automatic backups for SQL Server in the VM. This capability uses a feature that has been in the product for several releases using SQL Server Agent and backup to URL. You can learn more at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/virtual-machines/windows/automated-backup. While I do recommend you look at this feature, I found in the cloud customers typically build their own automated backup system (but use Azure Storage with backup to URL) or use the Azure Backup service.

High Availability

This option helps you configure Always On Availability Groups with SQL Server on Azure VM. Learn more about this option in the section titled “HADR” later in this chapter.

SQL Best Practices Assessment

This option is a tool that allows you to scan the configuration of your SQL Server deployment and provide advice on settings that may be not optimal or follow best practices based on the common knowledge of the SQL community and Microsoft. You can run an assessment on demand or on a schedule. There is some great advice from this tool, so I recommend you give it a spin and see how it can help you. You can see the complete set of rules it uses at https://github.com/microsoft/sql-server-samples/blob/master/samples/manage/sql-assessment-api and learn more about the feature at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/virtual-machines/windows/sql-assessment-for-sql-vm.

Security

Security options include integration with services like Microsoft Entra and Microsoft Defender for Cloud.

Security Configure

This option allows you to enable integration with Azure Key Vault which can be a very nice option to use with features like Transparent Data Encryption (Bring Your Own Key).

You can also enable integration with Microsoft Entra (formerly Azure Active Directory). This option requires SQL Server 2022 and is an excellent solution to provide an alternative for authentication instead of using basic or SQL Authentication, especially because you don’t need a domain controller like you do for Windows Authentication. One great use for this capability is to use a managed identity, so you can build passwordless applications connecting to SQL Server. Learn more at https://learn.microsoft.com/sql/relational-databases/security/authentication-access/azure-ad-authentication-sql-server-overview.

Microsoft Defender for Cloud

This capability used to be known as Advanced Data Security but is now wrapped under the Microsoft Defender umbrella. The capabilities here are still the same:

  • Vulnerability Assessment

    Scan for any configuration settings that could lead to a possible security vulnerability (e.g., sa account enabled) based on known industry standards (e.g., FedRAMP).

  • Advanced Threat Protection

    Alert to any threats detected like brute force login attacks (SQL auth enabled and someone tries to hack the sa password) and SQL injection attacks.

You can learn more about Microsoft Defender for Cloud and SQL on Azure VM at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/defender-for-cloud/defender-for-sql-usage.

Tip

There are two new capabilities from the Defender team worth looking at, including the Data Security Dashboard (https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/defender-for-cloud/data-aware-security-dashboard-overview) and a Copilot experience (https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/defender-for-cloud/copilot-security-in-defender-for-cloud).

There is one other option on the Service Menu worth looking at called SQL Server IaaS Agent Extension Settings. This can help you repair any issues with the extension and also set up auto upgrades.

In addition to using the portal, you can use the az sql vm CLI. This allows you to register your VM or change SQL Server properties. You can read all these options at https://learn.microsoft.com/cli/azure/sql/vm.

Configuring an Azure Virtual Machine

Inside the VM, your SQL Server configuration is completely identical to a virtual machine you deploy in your data center. There are some aspects to configuration of the virtual machine from the Azure infrastructure worth looking at.

Stopping vs. Deallocating

There could be several reasons to shut down your virtual machine in Azure. If you shut down the virtual machine inside the VM (e.g., Windows shutdown), the VM is stopped, but compute resources are reserved for the VM, which means you are still billed for compute. The VM status will show as Stopped in the portal and CLI interfaces. To shut down the VM and ensure you are not billed for compute, you need to use the interfaces outside the VM (such as Stop in the Command Menu of the Azure Portal or az CLI) to stop the VM. In this case, the status of the VM is listed as Stopped (deallocated). Depending on your requirements, you can even automate the process of starting and stopping VMs using Azure interfaces. Read more at https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/educator-developer-blog/azure-virtual-machine-auto-shutdown/ba-p/379342.

A related topic is maintenance windows for the infrastructure hosting virtual machines. Read more about updates and maintenance of Azure Virtual Machine hosts at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/virtual-machines/maintenance-and-updates.

Resizing

What if you did not pick the right Azure Virtual Machine size during deployment? You can use the Azure portal (choose Size from the Service Menu) or CLI interfaces to change the size of the virtual machine. This process is called resizing an Azure Virtual Machine. Resizing is much like you being able to change the resources available (CPU, storage, memory, etc.) for a VM within your data center. While you can resize a running Azure VM, a reboot of the VM is required for the resize operation. The sizes available to your choice to resize depend on whether your VM is running or stopped (deallocated). This difference is because there may be only certain sizes available on the current host of your VM (the running case). To see the full complete list of VM sizes, I recommend you stop (deallocate) your VM first. Read the overall Azure Virtual Machine resize story at https://azure.microsoft.com/blog/resize-virtual-machines/.

You can use Azure Migrate to move Azure Virtual Machines to other regions. Read more at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/site-recovery/azure-to-azure-tutorial-migrate. In addition, to change the resource group or subscription of your virtual machine, read the documentation at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-resource-manager/management/move-resource-group-and-subscription.

Security (RBAC)

Role-Based Access Control (RBAC) allows you to assign privileges to other Azure accounts to have permissions to manage the Azure Virtual Machine. Think of this as how to control security outside the VM. You manage this through the Access Control (IAM) option on the Service Menu. This is a common option for many Azure resources. Learn more at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/role-based-access-control/quickstart-assign-role-user-portal.

Other Config Options

There are other options to configure your Azure Virtual Machine using the portal from the Service Menu or CLI interfaces.

Examples include resetting the password for the admin user, viewing security recommendations, and redeploying the VM to another host. Read more about some of these options at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/virtual-machines/windows/tutorial-config-management.

Maximizing Storage Performance

Based on our experience with customers, getting the right size for your Azure Virtual Machine is the number one factor in ensuring you have the right performance you need for SQL Server (except for standard SQL Server performance optimization practices). Let’s go into more detail as why this choice is so important.

Best Practices

You saw earlier in this chapter the example of how a SQL Server gallery image will create additional managed data disks based on Azure Premium Storage. To achieve your best, I/O performance, and availability SQL Server on Azure Virtual Machine, you should keep these principles in mind, some of which I have mentioned as I showed you how to configure storage in the Azure Portal:

  • Use the Azure Portal to help guide your choices as it includes warnings if the VM size doesn’t support your I/O choices. It also helps you easily configure multiple disks (which use Windows Storage Spaces behind the scenes), separate your data and log storage, move system databases off the OS drive, and configure tempdb storage.

  • Do not create any user databases on the OS disk.

  • Put tempdb on the local SSD drive which is also called temporary storage. If your VM fails over for any reason to a different host, the contents of tempdb are lost, but who cares since tempdb is recreated at startup time.

  • Separate your database and log files on different managed disks as you would with any SQL Server production deployment.

  • Use premium managed disks at minimum (also called Premium SSD). One option for extreme low latency is Ultra disks. Ultra disks are more expensive but for SQL production workload could be a needed choice for heavy write workloads where the latency of the transaction log is important. Learn more about how to configure this at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/virtual-machines/windows/storage-migrate-to-ultradisk.

Premium SSD v2

As you saw from the examples of configuring storage via the Azure Portal, it is possible you need to overprovision storage to achieve your IOPS and throughput needs. This is because these resource numbers are tied to the size of premium managed disks.

Premium SSD v2 for SQL (now in preview) provides a new method to control your IOPS and throughput independent of the size of disks.

We have a new Azure portal experience to use this new option. However, as of the time of the writing of this book, it requires you to deploy your VM with an Availability Zone, even for a single SQL instance. While this will not cause any specific issue for you, deploying an Azure VM in a single Availability Zone does not really gain you any extra availability. Availability Zones are typically only needed for virtual machines for HA scenarios like Always On Availability Groups.

If you make this choice on the Basics screen of the portal (under Availability Options), you will see a new option under the Storage Configuration under SQL Server Setting that looks like Figure 3-28.

Figure 3-28

Choosing the new Premium SSD v2 storage for SQL

When you select Change configuration, you now see a new portal experience to control size, IOPS, and throughput independently as seen in Figure 3-29.

Figure 3-29

The new Premium SSD v2 storage experience

Learn more details at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/virtual-machines/windows/storage-configuration-premium-ssd-v2.

Learn More

You of course must balance these choices with cost. All the managed disks are based on Azure Blob Storage. An entire chapter would be needed to give you all the internals of how Azure Blob Storage and managed disks work. For now, look at these resources:

  • Details on Azure managed disk types can be found at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/virtual-machines/disks-types. You will see in these tables what sizes, IOPS, and throughput each type supports.

  • We have guidance in our documentation specific to choosing the right storage for SQL Server for Azure VM at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/virtual-machines/windows/performance-guidelines-best-practices-checklist.

  • This documentation provides nice guidance on choosing the right sizes for Azure Premium Storage along with VM size. You can read this at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/virtual-machines/premium-storage-performance.

  • An independent study on performance for SQL Server on Azure Virtual Machine was published by GigaOm. Learn more at https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/sql-server-blog/sql-server-on-azure-vms-the-best-price-performance-gets-even/ba-p/3727091.

One major key point I’ve mentioned before but worth repeating is to match the right VM size to meet your storage needs as VM sizes have limits on the number of data disk, total IOPS, and throughput across all disks. Then provision the data disks you need for database and transaction log files within that total IOPS.

Performance Monitoring

Since SQL Server is deployed inside a virtual machine, you should use all the normal techniques available to you to monitor performance, including SQL tools such as Dynamic Management Views (DMVS) and operating system tools such as Windows Performance Monitor or Linux tools.

Having said that, Azure Monitor provides integrated performance metrics for virtual machines, including SQL Server performance counters (for Windows only).

Azure Metrics

You can get rich metrics from Azure available in the Monitoring option in the Service Menu. By default, you get metrics for the host of the VM. Most likely though, you will want metrics for inside (a.k.a. guest) the VM. In order to enable this, you will need to select Diagnostic Settings from the Service Menu of the VM (not the SQL Virtual Machine), provision a new Azure Storage account in the same region as the VM (see these instructions on how to create one quickly: https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/storage/common/storage-account-create), and then select Enable guest-level monitoring.

After you enable guest-level monitoring, you will be presented with various options, including the ability to add specific counters for the guest VM. Under the Performance Counters option, you can select SQL Server counters. Once you have applied this, you can now use the Metric option in the Service Menu in the portal to see fundamental metrics like you would inside the VM with Windows Performance Monitor. Here is an example from my VM as seen in Figure 3-30.

Figure 3-30

Azure guest VM metrics for a virtual machine

You can also select the Logs option from the Service Menu to view Azure Metrics by using Kusto Query Language (KQL) queries. This allows you to view metrics from Log Analytics that can span more than the 14-day default of using Azure Metrics from the portal. Learn more at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-monitor/vm/tutorial-monitor-vm-guest#view-logs.

Insights

When you selected Enabled guest-level monitoring, Azure Monitor started collecting metrics in a Log Analytics workspace. By choosing the Insights option from the Service Menu, you can look at another visualization of these metrics. You can see an example of these metrics in Figure 3-31.

Figure 3-31

Insights for Azure Virtual Machine using Log Analytics

You can go even farther using a concept called Workbooks, which you can read more about at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-monitor/vm/vminsights-workbooks.

Networking

When I deployed the Azure Virtual Machine earlier in this chapter, several types of network resources were created in the resource group, including a virtual network, network interface, public IP address, and network security group.

The public IP address is important as it allows you to use tools like Remote Desktop to connect to the virtual machine. The network interface is the interconnection between the VM and the virtual network. The network security group (NSG) provides an Access Control List (ACL) for rules to allow or deny network traffic to the VM (like a firewall). You can read the complete picture of networks and Azure Virtual Machines at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/virtual-network/network-overview#network-security-groups.

Previously, I connected to the virtual machine using the Remote Desktop Client (RDP) which used the public IP address and access to the RDP port (3389). There could be other methods you want to use to connect to the virtual machine.

I recommend against enabling the SQL Server port 1433 to be open to the public Internet. However, that is the only method for you to connect a SQL Server client application or tool (like SSMS) to the SQL Server instance in the Azure Virtual Machine unless you run the application or tool in a computer that is connected to the virtual network of the VM. One method to do this is to deploy the application or another VM in Azure and join the same Azure virtual network. You can also deploy in another Azure virtual network and set up a configuration called virtual network peering, which you can read about at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/virtual-network/virtual-network-peering-overview.

Finally, you may find yourself wanting to connect on-premises resources such as applications or other computer or virtual machines to the virtual network for the Azure VM. You can read more about the details of setting up a configuration like this at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/architecture/reference-architectures/hybrid-networking.

HADR

No matter where you deploy SQL Server, almost everyone wants high availability at some level and the ability to execute, when necessary, disaster recovery techniques. Azure provides methods to deliver several choices for HADR.

Azure Storage

In the example in this chapter where I showed you how to deploy SQL Server in Azure Virtual Machine, I used data disks based on premium managed disks from Azure Storage. By default, managed disks have built-in redundancy called Locally Redundant Storage (LRS). LRS maintains three copies of data within the data center region. You can read more about Azure Storage redundancy at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/storage/common/storage-redundancy. You can actually make your disk zone redundant, but because the data is not replicated across zones, you could see a performance impact.

Backups

Since you are running SQL Server in the Azure infrastructure, you will no doubt want to store backups of SQL Server database and transaction logs within Azure. You could store your backups using T-SQL to a separate data disk using managed disks. However, another option is to store your backups to Azure Storage accounts using the backup to URL capability of SQL Server. You can read more about SQL Server backup to URL at https://learn.microsoft.com/sql/relational-databases/backup-restore/sql-server-backup-to-url. I mentioned earlier the option of using automatic backups from the Azure Portal which uses backup to URL. An advantage of using URL backups is you can configure your Azure Storage account to use a higher level of availability called geo-redundant storage (GRS). View the Service-Level Agreement (SLA) for Azure Storage accounts at https://www.microsoft.com/licensing/docs/view/Service-Level-Agreements-SLA-for-Online-Services.

Another option is to integrate backups for SQL Server with the Azure Backup service. Azure Backup offers a streaming-based service to back up SQL Server to Azure Storage. Azure Backup involves a VM extension that will use the Virtual Device Interface (VDI) APIs with SQL Server for backups. Read more about using Azure Backup with SQL Server at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/backup/backup-azure-sql-database.

The final option for backups in Azure uses file snapshot backups. File snapshot backups can be extremely fast. They require storing SQL Server data files directly to an Azure Storage account vs. a managed disk. You can read more about SQL Server file snapshot backups in Azure at https://learn.microsoft.com/sql/relational-databases/backup-restore/file-snapshot-backups-for-database-files-in-azure.

One other concept to consider is Azure File Share. Azure File Shares are like a network file share you might have in your organization but available to any virtual machine (think of an SMB-based network file share). Any file stored on this file share is available even if the VM is not available. An option with Azure File Shares is called a premium file share, which can support up to 100TB.

Tip

Since you have premium managed disk attached to the VM, you could also back up your databases to one of those disks locally (like backup to the G: drive). However, these disks are only available when attached to a VM. Using the other methods listed in this section allows you to access your backups independent of the VM being available.

Go Further with Azure Availability

Azure Virtual Machine is part of the Azure infrastructure that provides for availability during unplanned and planned events. For example, if an unplanned hardware problem occurs, Azure can use live migration technologies to failover your virtual machine to a healthy host. Azure also may update the underlying hosts of your virtual machine which may or may not require a reboot. You can read more about unplanned and planned downtime for Azure Virtual Machines at https://docs.microsoft.com/azure/virtual-machines/windows/manage-availability#understand-vm-reboots---maintenance-vs-downtime.

Azure offers more options to go further with high availability for Azure Virtual Machines which I’ve mentioned earlier in this book but worth repeating:

  1. Availability Set – Spreads your VMs across multiple fault and update domains in the same data center. Basically, this makes sure your VMs are spread across different racks and switches. The SLA is 99.95%.

     

  2. Availability Zone – Think of this as spreading an Availability Set across data centers within a region. The SLA is 99.99% but can cost more.

     

Unfortunately, you need to make this choice when you deploy. You can change it later, but you must migrate your VM to do it.

These choices are required if you plan to use an HADR solution such as an Always On Failover Cluster Instance or Availability Group. It does not make sense to use either of these options for a single VM deployment.

Always On Failover Cluster Instance

An Always On Failover Cluster Instance (FCI) provides high availability for SQL Server through shared storage. Since this is a virtual machine, using an FCI will be very much like a normal SQL Server. Having said that, consider these important concepts to use to make the deployment of an FCI successful in Azure:

  • You will need to deploy any VM in the cluster using an Availability Set or Availability Zone.

  • For shared storage for the cluster, consider Azure Shared Disks. Azure Shared Disks are specifically designed to share managed disks across virtual machines for scenarios like clusters. See more at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/virtual-machines/disks-shared.

  • Every Windows Failover Cluster Solution (WFCS) needs a witness for quorum. Azure provides a specific type of witness for the cloud called a cloud witness. Think of a cloud witness as a managed witness. You create the cloud witness, and it handles everything you need. Behind the scenes, it uses a VM and Azure Storage. Learn more at https://learn.microsoft.com/windows-server/failover-clustering/deploy-cloud-witness?tabs=windows#what-is-cloud-witness.

  • You need some type of network abstraction for listeners. Azure has the ability for you to create a multi-subnet virtual network to avoid the need for load balancers or distributed network names. Learn more at https://learn.microsoft.com/sql/sql-server/failover-clusters/windows/sql-server-multi-subnet-clustering-sql-server.

For a complete tutorial to build an FCI, start here and then follow the next step with each article: https://learn.microsoft.com/sql/sql-server/failover-clusters/install/before-installing-failover-clustering.

Always On Availability Groups

Always On Availability Groups (AG) increase your high availability RPO (Recovery Point Objective) and RTO (Recovery Time Object) using replicas. It is the flagship HADR capability for SQL Server using a non-shared storage concept with replicas.

We know that setting up an AG on Azure can be a daunting process. In fact, the complete tutorial to set up an AG with auto failover on Windows is documented at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/virtual-machines/windows/availability-group-manually-configure-tutorial-single-subnet.

Fortunately, our team built a new experience in the Azure Portal to make this easier. Assuming you first set up a Windows Domain Controller and a virtual network and then use a new Azure portal experience to deploy the rest of the solution, including failover clustering and an AG.

If you can remember earlier in the chapter, I showed you the experience to pick an Azure SQL solution like in Figure 3-32.

Figure 3-32

Choosing the HA option when deploying a SQL Azure VM

Notice the selection of high availability here. After picking your image choice and selecting Create, you will have a similar experience as I have shown you earlier in this chapter, except now you have choices to deploy multiple VMs and configure your cluster and AG.

For example, as you can see in Figure 3-33, you have choices in the Basics screen to deploy multiple VMs.

Figure 3-33

Basics for deploying a SQL HA deployment

In the Networking screen, you have options to set up a multi-subnet, including Listener IP addresses. Notice also an option for WSFC and Credentials. When you select SQL Server Settings, you now have options to configure the AG to deploy as you see in Figure 3-34.

Figure 3-34

SQL Server Settings to configure an AG for SQL Server Azure VM

To go through this complete experience, see https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/virtual-machines/windows/availability-group-azure-portal-configure.

One other scenario to consider is to set up a secondary replica for an AG in Azure from an on-premises AG primary replica. You can get more guidance on how to set this up at https://learn.microsoft.com/previous-versions/azure/virtual-machines/windows/sqlclassic/virtual-machines-windows-classic-sql-onprem-availability. And if you set up an async secondary replica to be passive, you can tag this VM for HA purposes and avoid SQL licensing costs.

SQL Server and Linux High Availability

SQL Server on Linux supports Always On Failover Cluster Instance and Availability Groups functionality through the database engine. Automatic failover capabilities are often supported through Linux packages like Pacemaker, DH2i, or HPE ServiceGuard.

To see the complete story of deploying an Always On Availability Group on Linux, see https://learn.microsoft.com/sql/linux/sql-server-linux-availability-group-ha.

Microsoft Innovations and Azure Boost

Microsoft is always looking to improve the infrastructure that hosts Azure Virtual Machines. One of these innovations is called Azure Boost.

Azure Boost is a system designed by Microsoft that offloads server virtualization processes traditionally performed by the hypervisor and host OS onto purpose-built software and hardware.

The great thing about Azure Boost is that you just need to choose a virtual machine size that supports Boost (which you can find at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-boost/overview#current-availability), and all the goodness is delivered behind the scenes on hosts.

The main benefits of using a virtual machine size that supports Boost are faster networking, faster storage with lower latency, higher IOPS/throughput, a more secure host, and higher performance because CPU resources can be more dedicated to the guest virtual machines. Geek out on all the details at https://aka.ms/azureboost.

Summary
