You have learned in this chapter how to deploy and configure SQL Server with Azure Virtual Machine looking at many different options as part of deployment. You have also learned unique aspects of security, performance, and HADR related to SQL Server and Azure VM. Azure VM provides a great infrastructure for running SQL Server and is the easiest way to migrate your SQL Server to the cloud.

Try out your knowledge of what you have learned in this chapter by going through the following labs in my Cloud Workshop for the SQL Professional GitHub repo (https://aka.ms/cloudsqlworkshop):

https://github.com/microsoft/cloudsqlworkshop/tree/main/cloudsqlworkshop/03_Deploy_SQL_AzureVM

https://github.com/microsoft/cloudsqlworkshop/tree/main/cloudsqlworkshop/04_Manage_and_Optimize_SQL_AzureVM

Now that you have studied SQL Server on Azure VM, it is time to show you how to deploy and configure Azure SQL Database and Managed Instance.

4. Deploying Azure SQL

Deploying an Azure SQL Managed Instance or Database is a different but similar experience than deploying SQL Server on Azure Virtual Machine. The experience is the same because you can use the Azure portal and CLI. The difference is that Azure is managing the virtual machine and infrastructure, so there are several of the options you pick for a virtual machine you do not have to worry about.

In this chapter, you will learn the options and process to deploy and connect to an Azure SQL Managed Instance and Database. You will also learn the options to migrate existing databases into Azure SQL. In addition, you will learn some implementation details of the architecture used to host Azure SQL Managed Instances and Databases.

You have the option to follow along with the examples in this chapter. You will need the following to complete these examples:

  • An Azure subscription.

  • A minimum of Contributor role access to the Azure subscription. You can read more about Azure built-in roles at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/role-based-access-control/built-in-roles.

  • Access to the Azure Portal (web or Windows application).

  • Installation of the az CLI (see https://learn.microsoft.com/cli/azure/install-azure-cli for more details). You can also use the Azure Cloud Shell instead since az is already installed. You can read more about the Azure Cloud Shell at https://azure.microsoft.com/get-started/azure-portal/cloud-shell.

  • You will run some T-SQL in this chapter, so install a tool like SQL Server Management Studio (SSMS) at https://aka.ms/ssms.

I also have a link to self-paced labs you can go through at the end of this chapter.

Pre-deployment Planning

Before you jump into deploying an Azure SQL Database or Managed Instance, I recommend you spend some time doing some pre-deployment planning. Reviewing your choices and making a few informed decisions will save you time and effort.

New Deployment or Migration

One of the first decisions to make is whether you plan to migrate an existing database or instance or deploy a new database or instance. The process of deploying will be the same, but migrating implies you need to assess your current SQL Server instance, database, or other database environments before you deploy. Your assessment will give you guidance on what type of deployment choice you need to make based on your current requirements. You must decide what possible changes must be made to your application, schema, scripts, or other aspects to your current deployment with SQL Server or other database platforms. You also must consider how to migrate your actual data into the new deployment. This chapter will include sections specifically geared around what consideration and tools you can use to migrate to Azure SQL Managed Instance and Database. Here are some great resources for you to consider as you think about migration:

  • Azure cloud migration and modernization center – Here is a central resource of information and access to Microsoft experts to migrate any resources to Azure. Check out https://azure.microsoft.com/solutions/migration.

  • The Microsoft Data Migration Guide – Get step-by-step guidance on migrating to Azure for any type of data. Learn more at https://aka.ms/datamigration.

  • SQL Server enabled by Azure Arc migration assessment – In preview at the time of the writing of this book, this capability allows you to connect your existing SQL Server to Azure with Arc and get a continuous assessment on what is the best solution to migrate to Azure, including resource (sizing) recommendations. Learn more at https://learn.microsoft.com/sql/sql-server/azure-arc/migration-assessment.

  • Azure Database Migration Service (DMS) – This Azure service will allow to perform both offline and online migrations to Azure SQL Managed Instance and Database (offline only). Learn more at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/dms/dms-overview.

  • Azure Migrate – Use this service to assess and migrate (using DMS) at scale. Learn more at https://aka.ms/azuremigrate.

  • Managed Instance link – While DMS provides an online migration solution, it uses a log shipping approach. A possible better online migration path is Azure SQL Managed Instance link. This allows you to migrate a SQL Server 2016, 2019, or 2022 instance to Azure SQL Managed Instance using the power of Always On Availability Groups. Learn more at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/managed-instance-link-feature-overview?view=azuresql#migrate-to-azure.

Making Deployment Choices

Whether you are migrating or creating a new deployment, you have several choices to make that are worth the time to plan out. Chapter 2 of this book is invaluable to go back and read as it describes choices and differences between Azure SQL Managed Instance and Azure SQL Database.

Having said that, let us review some important choices at a high level that can affect your decision-making:

  • If you need SQL Server instance features like SQL Server Agent, Database Mail, and cross-database queries, Managed Instance is the choice you need to make.

  • If your database size is > 32TB, your only choice as of the time of writing this book is Azure SQL Database Hyperscale.

Past these two choices, either Azure SQL Managed Instance or Database likely meets your needs. However, as I called out in Chapter 2 of the book, there can be advantages in using Azure SQL Database because Microsoft will manage both the infrastructure and the SQL Server instance to let you focus on the database. Furthermore, Azure SQL Database can offer you more options such as Serverless compute and elastic pools.

The options you will pick as you deploy a Managed Instance or Database can be thought of the following as listed in Figure 4-1 (credits to my colleague Anna Hoffman for this diagram).

Figure 4-1

Deployment choices for Azure SQL

Deploying for Free

If you are looking for ways to deploy Azure SQL for free for development or proof of concept (POC) scenarios, consider the following options:

The Azure SQL Managed Instance Free Trial

I have heard so many customers ask me how they can do tests for Azure SQL Managed Instance to migrate a SQL Server without having to spend a lot of money. We now have a free 12-month trial for Azure SQL Managed Instance to now give you this capability. This is available for any Azure subscription. Learn more at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/free-offer.

The Azure SQL Database Free Offer

SQL Server Developer Edition is so popular because developers can develop and test their application for free. We now have a similar option for Azure SQL Database, and it is not a trial. For the lifetime of your Azure subscription, you can now deploy a free Azure SQL Database. Learn more at https://aka.ms/freedboffer.

Deployment Methods

You can deploy an Azure Managed Instance or Database using the Azure Portal or through a CLI with the az utility, PowerShell, or even REST APIs (az rest can be used to help you).

If you are just trying out Azure SQL or doing a POC, you can easily use the Azure Portal. However, for a repeatable process to deploy (imagine if you needed to redeploy a Managed Instance exactly as it was originally configured), then a script using a CLI is a better option. Remember you also can use Azure templates to help automate deployments. You can read more about using Azure templates for Azure SQL at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/arm-templates-content-guide?view=azuresql&tabs=single-database and https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/arm-templates-content-guide?view=azuresql&tabs=managed-instance.

You also have the option to use Bicep to deploy Azure SQL Managed Instance (https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/create-bicep-quickstart) or Azure SQL Database (https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/single-database-create-bicep-quickstart).

Terraform is also supported both for Azure SQL Managed Instance (https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/instance-create-terraform) and Azure SQL Database (https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/single-database-create-terraform-quickstart).

Let me show you another method. I mentioned the Copilot for Azure experience in Chapter 2 of the book. Here is an example in Figure 4-2 of how to ask Copilot to help provide an example of how to deploy an Azure SQL Database using the az CLI.

Figure 4-2

Using Copilot in Azure to help deploy an Azure SQL Database

Note

You can see here that after providing responses Copilot provides suggestions for other things you may want to do. At this point, I have not deployed a database, so Copilot has no context. You will see later in the book more sophisticated experiences after you deploy the database. However, as you go through this chapter, you can ask Copilot different questions related to topics you see in the chapter. It can look up concepts in the documentation and provide examples in many cases faster than you probably can search for yourself.

Deployment Option

I discussed earlier whether you will consider Azure SQL Managed Instance or Database. Within each of these options, there is a choice of using a pool. As I mentioned in Chapter 2 of the book, Azure SQL Managed Instance offers a Managed Instance pool which could be a better fit for a smaller, cost-effective Managed Instance. Deployment times are also much faster with pools. You can read more about Managed Instance pools at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/instance-pools-overview. I’ll discuss the use of Managed Instance pools in several places later in this chapter.

Note

Managed Instance pools at the time of the writing of this book went through a public preview refresh. It was in public preview for a long time, so many thought it would never make General Availability, but it is on the right path now in my opinion.

As I mentioned in both Chapters 1 and 2 of this book, Azure SQL Database provides an option called an elastic pool. An elastic pool can be a good choice if you plan to use Azure SQL Database to host many databases. ISVs and Software as a Service (SaaS) developers often look at this choice to save costs and manage databases more efficiently. I’ll discuss more about elastic pools in several places later in the chapter.

Region

Choosing a specific Azure region can be important just as I described in Chapter 3 on virtual machines. You need to make sure your deployment options are available in your Azure region choice.

You may have certain compliance and security requirements which also dictate what region you choose.

You may be implementing specific HADR options like Availability Zones, geo-replication, or auto-failover groups and have specific regions in mind to make those deployment successful. Remember I talked about in Chapter 2 of the book the concept called paired regions, which are important to make these geo-region choices.

If you need to move an Azure SQL Managed Instance or Database to another region, read a checklist in our documentation at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/move-resources-across-regions.

Azure SQL Database is a “Ring 0” service which means it gets deployed in every region as a default service. Managed Instance is not exactly at that status yet, but it is generally available in all regions.

In addition, you need to consider where your application will be hosted and latency requirements between where the application will be hosted and your Azure SQL deployment. Consider performance and proximity to other services. I was chatting with my colleague Anna Hoffman on this topic. She said “…but I feel that it’s not just where the application is – where are the users? Where should the application be? If you have geo-replication or auto failover groups, how do you build a globally available solution?”

Purchasing Model

For Azure SQL Database only, you will need to choose a purchasing model. The choices are DTU or vCore. I explained these models and the history behind them in Chapters 1 and 2 of the book. While the DTU model may be a choice for you, I recommend the vCore model. You have more choices with vCore and licensing options such as Azure Hybrid Benefit (AHB). The vCore model gives you more transparency and control over the underlying resources (CPU, memory, storage), making it easier to match your database’s performance needs.

If you select the DTU model and want to move to the vCore model at a later date, consult the documentation at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/migrate-dtu-to-vcore.

Service Tier (SLO)

If Azure SQL Managed Instance is your deployment option, then you will need to select a service tier (we also refer to this as the Service-Level Objective or SLO) of General Purpose (GP) or Business Critical (BC). I described these service tier options in Chapter 2 of the book. While resource limits and performance may differ, one of the primary differences with these tiers is how availability works which you will learn more about in Chapter 8 of the book. One notable difference between Business Critical is that it supports In-Memory OLTP capabilities. Remember in Chapter 2 I talked about the “NextGen” of GP for Managed Instance. Be sure to look at that option because it gives you more options and more powerful storage performance. A comparison between GP and BC for Managed Instance can be found at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/resource-limits.

Tip

You will see later in this chapter that it can often take time to deploy a Managed Instance. Changing between GP and BC is possible but could result in significant downtime. You can learn more about specific times at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/management-operations-overview. Managed Instance does support a concept called fast provisioning for GP service tiers with certain configurations. You can even provision them in 30 minutes. Learn more at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/management-operations-overview?view=azuresql#fast-provisioning.

If Azure SQL Database is your deployment option, then you also have the choice of General Purpose (GP) vs. Business Critical (BC) service tiers. In addition, you have the choice of Hyperscale. If you choose General Purpose or Hyperscale, you also have the choice of Provisioned vs. Serverless. This is also called a compute model or tier. I covered all these options in Chapter 2 of the book, but I’ll also cover this topic in this chapter.

Azure SQL Database Hyperscale I believe will start becoming your default choice for databases. I talked about the history of Hyperscale (Project Socrates) in Chapter 1 of the book. If you read the first edition of this book, you probably walked away with the impression that Hyperscale is only for the largest database deployments. And that is our fault not yours for that impression. Several things have changed since 2020 for Hyperscale:

  • Hyperscale now supports serverless.

  • Hyperscale now supports elastic pools.

  • The price of Hyperscale in late 2023 was significantly reduced. Learn more about this at https://azure.microsoft.com/updates/general-availability-lower-simplified-pricing-for-azure-sql-database-hyperscale-expected-in-december-2023.

One key point about Hyperscale is that storage is autoscaled! You never specify how much database space you need. We just auto grow per your needs.

I believe Hyperscale now becomes a starting point for anyone and just let auto-scaling for computer (serverless) and storage flex as your application needs grow or change. Read all the details about Hyperscale at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/service-tier-hyperscale.

And speaking of Serverless (which I talked about in Chapter 1 of the book), it is a unique option we have created for auto-scale and scenarios where your database may not always be utilized. It provides a new cost-effective way to deploy and use an Azure SQL Database. Read more about Serverless at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/serverless-tier-overview.

Switching between GP and BC for Azure SQL Database is typically significantly faster than with Managed Instance. You can also switch between Serverless and Provisioned easily. For Hyperscale, you can switch back to a GP service tier. Learn more at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/manage-hyperscale-database.

Hardware

Even though for Azure SQL we abstract you from the infrastructure and virtualization used for the deployment, we provide options for a hardware generation.

For Azure SQL Managed Instance, you have three choices:

  • The Standard series is called Gen5 (it used to be Gen4 as we continually improve our generation of hardware). It has great choices for the number of vCores and memory per vCore.

  • The premium-series uses a newer generation of processors and offers more vCores and a higher memory per vCore.

  • The memory-optimized premium-series is like the premium-series but offers a higher memory per vCore. This option for General Purpose doesn’t support as many vCores as the premium-series.

Keep up with all the details at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/resource-limits?view=azuresql#hardware-configuration-characteristics.

For Azure SQL Database, you have the following options:

  • The Standard series is also called Gen5 (it used to be Gen4 as we continually improve our generation of hardware). It has great choices for the number of vCores and memory per vCore.

  • The Fsv2-series has the fastest CPU speeds, but you cannot provision as many vCores as you can for Gen5. This choice is only available for the General Purpose service tier.

  • The DC-series only supports 8 vCores but is critical for security as it supports the Intel Software Guard Extension (Intel SGX) for secure enclaves used with Always Encrypted.

  • Hyperscale supports premium and premium memory-optimized choices for more vCores and a higher memory to vCore ratio.

Keep up with all the details for these choices at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/service-tiers-sql-database-vcore?view=azuresql&tabs=azure-portal#hardware-configuration.

vCore and Storage Sizes

Once you have figured these options, you have choices on storage size. The DTU mode for Azure SQL Database has a DTU number you can choose (and a data size). For the vCore purchasing model, you have both number of vCores and database storage max size to select (remember you don’t need this for Hyperscale). There are a few differences in how these options work depending on your other choices. I will describe these differences as I walk you through the deployment process in the rest of this chapter.

Price

Just like with Azure Virtual Machine, take advantage of the Azure Pricing Calculator to plug in some of these choices to get an idea of your costs. This includes using Azure Hybrid Benefit. You can find the pricing calculator for Azure SQL Managed Instance at https://azure.microsoft.com/pricing/details/azure-sql/sql-managed-instance/single/ and Azure SQL Database at https://azure.microsoft.com/pricing/calculator/?service=sql-database.

Tip

To make sure to find Azure SQL Database and Managed Instance, search for Azure SQL Database. From there, you can change type for Managed Instance.

Figure 4-3 shows an example of the pricing calculator for Azure SQL Database.

Figure 4-3

The pricing calculator for Azure SQL Database

Consider Resource Limits

Your choices from deployment options, service tier, and sizes can affect your resource limits. These are the following resource limits to consider as you make these choices. I call these out because these may not be obvious as you deploy through the Azure Portal:

  • Max memory

  • Max log size

  • Log rate governance

  • IOPS and I/O latency

  • Max size of tempdb

  • Max concurrent workers

  • Backup retention

I will discuss more about log rate governance and IOPS and I/O latency in Chapter 7 of this book. For now, keep these concepts in mind as they can affect the performance of applications such as those that are heavy transaction log workloads.

To see the specific limits for Azure SQL Managed Instance, please see these well-documented tables at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/resource-limits. To see the specific limits for Azure SQL Database, please see the table at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/resource-limits-vcore-single-databases.

You should also know that there are overall Azure SQL limits per subscription per region. You can view what these limits are at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/resource-limits and https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/resource-limits-logical-server.

It is possible to make a request to Microsoft to increase your subscription limits. This is called a quota increase request. Read more at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/quota-increase-request.

Deploying Azure SQL Managed Instance

Similar to the process I documented in Chapter 3 for a virtual machine, deploying an Azure SQL Managed Instance through the Azure Portal starts by using the Azure SQL option from the Azure Marketplace (I showed you this view in Figure 3-1).

Using the three Azure SQL choices, you would select SQL Managed Instance and single instance, then click Create.

Walking Through a Deployment

Let us walk through each screen with deployment options of a Managed Instance and then deploy it through the Azure Portal. I’m going to use the new NextGen General Purpose service tier as an example to walk through the deployment experience. You will see a familiar but slightly different experience as you saw in Chapter 3 for SQL Server on Azure Virtual Machine.

As I mentioned earlier in the chapter about pre-deployment planning, I highly recommend you take the time to review all the options I’m going to show you. This is because some of the choices you will make can only be made at deployment and cannot be modified (which would require you to migrate afterward). In addition, some choices can be modified (like service tier) but may require some downtime.

Basics

Figure 4-4 shows the fields you complete on the top of the Basics screen, including choosing Subscription, Resource Group, Instance Name (this becomes part of the server name and @@SERVERNAME of the instance), Region, Pool, and Compute+Storage.

Figure 4-4

Basics top of screen to deploy Azure SQL Managed Instance

I like the fact that the estimated cost is listed on the right side as I make all my choices. This is a common experience we provide for Azure SQL.

Notice the option called Compute + Storage. This is where you will choose Service Tier and Size (vCores + Storage). Notice for me the default is General Purpose with 8 vCores and 256GB max storage. Click Configure Managed Instance to see what your options are, which should look like Figure 4-5.

Figure 4-5

Top of screen for Compute+Storage for Azure SQL Managed Instance

This is an important screen to make choices for your deployment, so let’s look at these options.

At the top of this screen, you can see you can choose General Purpose (GP), the default, or Business Critical (BC), and some of the resource limits and performance expectations are listed with each. From pre-deployment planning, remember there are other significant reasons to choose BC, including

  • Access to the In-Memory OLTP feature

  • Higher availability because BC uses local storage and a replica architecture

Next, you will notice I have enabled the NexGen GP option. As this feature becomes generally available, I expect this to be the default GP behavior.

Now I can choose the hardware generation which I’ve described these choices earlier in the chapter.

Now I can choose my “size” options, including vCores, max storage, and IOPS (this is one of the new features of NextGen).

Your hardware generation choice dictates your possible max vCore values which can be up to 128.

The maximum storage value is called the maximum instance size and is the maximum size allowed for all database and transaction log files associated with databases for the managed instance. You should think of this as the maximum storage drive for your databases. The maximum storage size is different depending on your vCore choice and choice of GP and BC. GP allows for up to 32TB, and BC allows for up to 16TB.

Note

I discussed the architecture of Business Critical deployments in Chapter 2 of the book and will elaborate more on this architecture in Chapter 8. The reason for the lower storage limit for BC is the fact that databases are stored on local SSD drives vs. Azure Storage.

Tempdb max sizes are dependent on vCore selections but are counted toward the overall maximum storage limit. In fact, all databases, including system databases, count toward the overall max instance storage size.

You can run the following query after you deploy your managed instance (in any database context) to see how much space your databases are taking up in relation to the max storage for the instance: 

SELECT TOP 1 used_storage_gb = storage_space_used_mb/1024,
max_storage_size_gb = reserved_storage_mb/1024
FROM sys.server_resource_stats ORDER BY start_time DESC;

Scrolling down, you can see the rest of the options to choose from in Figure 4-6.

Figure 4-6

Other choices for Compute+Storage for Azure SQL Managed Instance

Your first choice is zone redundancy. That option is disabled during the preview of NextGen GP but will be available once this is generally available. Zone redundancy uses Availability Zones to make your deployment more highly available. I highly recommend you consider this option for your deployments. I will discuss this concept more in Chapter 8 of the book.

You also have a choice to save money using your existing SQL Server licenses with Azure Hybrid Benefit (AHB). I’ll pause and say the Hybrid failover rights apply to a Managed Instance that is used for disaster recovery. Learn more in Chapter 8 of the book.

You also have choices for the redundancy of your backups. Unless you have specific data residency requirements, I recommend geo-redundant backups which replicate your backups in Azure Storage to known paired regions. I’ll discuss more about backup redundancy and costs in Chapter 8 of the book.

Click Apply after you make any changes (Apply is only enabled if you change the defaults; you can click the X in the upper right-hand corner to get back to the Basics screen).

Tip

Hitting Apply will not deploy the Managed Instance yet, but take your time to get your choices here as close to correct as possible. Why? You can change them later, but Managed Instance changes to tiers and sizes can be a long operation.

We still have one more choice on the Basics screen as you can see in Figure 4-7.

Figure 4-7

Authentication choices for Azure SQL Managed Instance during deployment

This option allows you to choose Microsoft Entra authentication only, mixed, or just SQL Authentication. If you choose any Entra option, you must specify an Entra account. These choices become a member of the sysadmin role for the instance. If you have never used Microsoft Entra authentication, it will require some learning for you. It is very similar to using Windows Authentication on SQL Server, but there are several differences. This capability is the most secure method and even supports passwordless applications.

Setting this up though for Azure SQL Managed Instance has one possible roadblock (which is why I didn’t use it here). You must have special permissions in your Microsoft Entra system to use this option (I don’t at Microsoft by default have this permission so have a special process to request it). Read more at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/authentication-aad-configure?view=azuresql&tabs=azure-powershell#provision-microsoft-entra-admin-sql-managed-instance.

Networking

Click Next: Networking > to review your networking choices. Your screen should look like Figure 4-8.

Figure 4-8

Azure SQL Managed Instance Networking Options

Virtual Network

You can see at the top of the screen that a new virtual network will be created to host the Azure SQL Managed Instance. One of the advantages of Managed Instance is that it is deployed in a private virtual network. You could deploy your own Azure virtual network first (you can use the Azure portal or CLI) and select that virtual network on this deployment screen. If you choose to use your own virtual network, you must configure it a specific way, which you can read at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/vnet-existing-add-subnet.

Tip

Did you know you can deploy multiple instances in the same virtual network? Doing this will speed up subsequent deployments, but make sure this meets your security and networking needs.

Connection Type

Notice on my screen I have chosen a connection type of Redirect. The default is proxy. A proxy connection requires that any connection to the Managed Instance by a tool or application (a connection to the TDS port 1433) must always go through a gateway. A redirect connection type uses the gateway to find the direct virtual private IP address of the node containing the Managed Instance. All subsequent traffic flows directly to the node. You can read more about these connection types at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/connection-types-overview. Proxy can be more secure, but redirect can be faster. The virtual network and included subnet will have all the appropriate Network Security Group (NSG) rules applied for these types if you choose to create the virtual network as part of this deployment step.

Public Endpoint

You have the option to enable TDS traffic on a public endpoint. The public endpoint will be enabled on port 3342 (and get redirected in the virtual network to the node instance port 1433). While I do not recommend using this option for production, it is one of the quickest ways to get connected to a Managed Instance. You can read more about the public endpoint for Managed Instance at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/public-endpoint-overview.

You also can control the minimum TLS connection encryption for the instance. I would always recommend using the latest TLS version. The only reason you may want to use a different one is for older applications where that version is not supported. Learn more at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/minimal-tls-version-configure.

Notice that accelerated networking is automatically enabled for the Managed Instance.

Security

Click Next: Security > to make further security selections as seen in Figure 4-9.

Figure 4-9

Security choices for Azure SQL Managed Instance

I’ve mentioned Microsoft Defender in previous chapters, and the same capabilities (but customized for Managed Instance) are available for vulnerability assessments and advanced threat protection.

You can set up your own user-defined managed identity for passwordless applications. By default, you get a system-defined managed identity.

If you want to use Windows Authentication in your applications against Azure SQL Managed Instance, you can enable an option for a Service Principal. If you want to avoid changing our application to use Microsoft Entra authentication, consider this option. Learn more at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/winauth-azuread-overview.

Transparent Data Encryption (TDE), which is encryption at rest for the storage of your databases and transaction logs, is enabled by default (you need to enable this for each individual database). This option allows you to set up your own key for encryption.

Additional Settings

Click Next: Additional settings > to enable a few additional options for the deployment as seen in Figure 4-10.

Figure 4-10

Additional settings for Managed Instance

The collation here is like setting a collation for a SQL Server. It is important to know that you cannot change the instance collation after it is supplied here.

Note

You can set and change database collations on Managed Instance after deployment. Just keep in mind having different database collations can cause issues for cross-database queries or temporary table operations.

The time zone is the recognized time zone by the SQL Server engine on the Managed Instance node. I have changed this to my local time zone, but it can be UTC or whatever time zone you want to choose. You cannot change this after deployment.

The Managed Instance can be part of a failover group which we will talk more about in Chapter 8 of the book. You will use that option when you review those topics later. For now, leave it to the default of No.

Maintenance window allows you to define a different timeframe when scheduled updates must be applied to your deployment. The default is any data between 5pm and 8am based on your time zone, or you can configure this for 10pm to 6am either Monday–Thursday or Friday–Sunday. You can change the maintenance window after you deploy.

Update policy is a relatively new concept. You can choose to keep your Managed Instance at a version compatible with SQL Server 2022. I’ll show you in Chapter 8 how you can take advantage of this for an online disaster recovery capability. However, if you choose this option, you will not be able to get new features that typically come from a versionless deployment (but you will have Cumulative Updates automatically applied). The first option ensures you are always up to date, but you will not be able to restore a backup or participate in an online disaster recovery scenario with SQL Server 2022.

I will choose the SQL Server 2022 update policy, so I can show you these new compatibility features later in the book.

Tags

Click Next: Tags > to define a tag like how I described in Chapter 3 with Azure Virtual Machines.

Deploy!

Click Next: Review + create > to view the final screen before deploying. Like with the deployment for Azure Virtual Machine, this screen shows estimated costs, terms of use, privacy policy, a review of all the options you have chosen, and the option to download an Azure template that describes these deployment options.

You will also see a warning that based on my choices the deployment could take as much as 6 hours to complete. For my deployment, it was completed in about 1 hour and 45 minutes. If you want to track more information during your deployment, you can use the Azure Portal (https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/management-operations-monitor).

So, click Create to deploy, leave this screen open to see the progress, and read on to the next section about deploying with a CLI and then some architecture and implementation details you might find interesting why the deployment runs.

Deploying with a CLI

An Azure Managed Instance can be deployed with command-line interfaces (CLI) through the az sql mi (https://learn.microsoft.com/cli/azure/sql/mi) command interfaces or through New-AzSQLInstance PowerShell cmdlet (https://learn.microsoft.com/powershell/module/az.sql/new-azsqlinstance).

I went down the path to build an example with az sql mi and found that I needed to run several az CLI commands to create the virtual network, subnet, and all associated settings. Therefore, I only recommend you use the az sql mi CLI with Azure templates. An example template can be found at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/create-template-quickstart?view=azuresql&tabs=azure-cli.

Tip

Just like I showed you how to use Copilot in Azure to create a database using the az CLI, it can also give you tips and examples on how to do this for Azure SQL Managed Instance. It also offers options to help you configure a virtual network and subnets.

PowerShell requires you to set up the virtual network and other context before executing New-AzSQLInstance. There is a good tutorial on using PowerShell at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/scripts/create-configure-managed-instance-powershell.

As I mentioned earlier in this chapter, you have options to deploy an Azure SQL Managed Instance with both Bicep and Terraform.

Implementation Details

Note

These implementation details may change over time as we change and improve the service. I offer up some of these details, so you can understand how we build, manage, and run the service.

Azure SQL Managed Instances are deployed on nodes (virtual machines) powered by Azure Service Fabric in a concept called a ring or virtual cluster. A virtual cluster is a dedicated set of isolated virtual machines that run in a virtual network subnet.

When you deploy your first managed instance in a new virtual network (subnet) as I did in my example, you are in fact deploying an entire virtual cluster. This explains why the initial deployment can take so long to complete. You can deploy other managed instances in the same virtual network subnet, and the deployment is slightly faster. Read more about specific expected times at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/management-operations-overview.

A managed instance is a full SQL Server engine database instance deployed in a dedicated virtual machine in the virtual cluster. Microsoft will decide how to deploy these virtual machines on various nodes of the cluster. It is possible that a node may have one virtual machine with an instance or multiple virtual machines. As per the promise of Platform as a Service (PaaS), Microsoft abstracts you from those details. Your interface with the Managed Instance is through either standard SQL Server interfaces such as T-SQL or Azure interfaces such as the portal, CLI, or REST API. You will never directly access the underlying virtual machines.

This architecture also explains why certain management operations such as scaling vCores can also take a long period of time as some of these operations require a deployment of a new virtual cluster with either attaching files from Azure Storage or reseeding a replica.

I described some of the architecture of General Purpose (GP) vs. Business Critical (BC) tiers in Chapter 2 of the book. I will describe them further in Chapter 8 of the book. Either of these service tiers use the same virtual cluster architecture just with different storage and HA implementations.

Resource limits for a Managed Instance such as memory limits, max storage size, and others are enforced through several mechanisms depending on your choices for deployment. For example, memory limits are enforced with Windows Job Objects (and you cannot configure “max server memory”). You can read more about Windows Job Objects at https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/procthread/job-objects. Storage capacity (or max size) is enforced by the File Server Resource Manager (FSRM), which you can read more about at https://learn.microsoft.com/windows-server/storage/fsrm/fsrm-overview.

A Managed Instance pool deployment can be far faster because an instance pool can be a set of SQL Server instances running in the same virtual machine. Isolation and resource limits are applied using Windows Job Objects. Figure 4-11 represents a visual comparing managed instance and pools. This figure comes from the documentation at https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/instance-pools-overview?view=azuresql#architecture.

Figure 4-11

The Managed Instance and pool architecture

Connecting and Verifying Deployment

Once your deployment is complete, your screen should look like Figure 4-12.

Figure 4-12

Deployment complete for Managed Instance

Let’s look at a few details on this screen:

  1. If you want a summary of the deployment, you can select Operation Details, which among other things can show you the duration of the deployment.

     

  2. You can get more details about each step of the deployment by selecting More events in the Activity Log.

     

  3. You can select Go to Resource to see your completed deployment in the Azure Portal.

     

If you click Go to Resource, you will see a screen like in Figure 4-13.

Figure 4-13

The Overview screen for Azure SQL Managed Instance

One of the first steps you will want to take at this point is try and connect to the new instance and verify the deployment. I like to verify my SQL Server installations with a set of simple T-SQL queries.

Connect to a Managed Instance

There are several different methods to connect to your Managed Instance, and you can see all of them at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/connect-application-instance.

What I personally like to do is connect with an Azure Virtual Machine inside the virtual network that was created as part of the deployment, so I don’t have to enable the public endpoint. I call this virtual machine a jumpbox. I’ll use RDP to connect to the VM, but in the VM, I use tools like SSMS to connect to the private network for the instance.

I’ll create a new Azure Virtual Machine (I just use a SQL Server Developer Edition from the marketplace) in the same resource group as my Managed Instance. During deployment of the VM, I’ll choose the virtual network created for the Managed Instance (usually vnet-<MI name>, so the name was vnet-bwsqlnextgeni). On this screen, I choose to manage subnet configuration. This allows me to create a new subnet called default (and use the default choices). This becomes the private subnet for applications like my VM to connect to the virtual network of the Managed Instance.

My network settings look like Figure 4-14.

Figure 4-14

Networking settings for VM jumpbox to Managed Instance

With my jumpbox now deployed, I will use SSMS inside the VM to connect to the Managed Instance. When you connect to a Managed Instance, the server’s name becomes a URL like this (which you can find on the Overview screen in the portal for the Managed Instance).

bwsqlminextgen.b243ea7f888c.database.windows.net

Figure 4-15 shows an example of SSMS connected to an Azure SQL Managed Instance.

Figure 4-15

SSMS connected to Azure SQL Managed Instance through a jumpbox

Verify the Deployment

Notice that Object Explorer in SSMS looks almost identical to a SQL Server except for the Fully Qualified Domain Name (FQDN) for the server name.

To verify the installation of SQL Server, I often use a few techniques, including running queries against system catalog views and DMVs, and look at the ERRORLOG.

Examining the ERRORLOG

You do not have access to the file system for the virtual machine hosting the Managed Instance. To look at the ERRORLOG, we will need a tool like SSMS or T-SQL.

You can use Object Explorer in SSMS to view the ERRORLOG, but I prefer just T-SQL, so I can execute sp_readerrorlog to look at the current ERRORLOG file. I must warn you that we dump all types of extra information in the ERRORLOG for a Managed Instance (yes, even more than a SQL Server), and it can take several seconds to execute. My colleague Dimitri Furman wrote a blog post with some sample code to filter down the ERRORLOG for a Managed Instance. You can view this at https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/datacat/azure-sql-db-managed-instance-sp-readmierrorlog/ba-p/305506.

There are a few key messages I look at startup in the ERRORLOG and found these in my Managed Instance: 

SQL Server detected 1 sockets with 4 cores per socket and 8 logical processors per socket, 8 total logical processors; using 8 logical processors based on SQL Server licensing. This is an informational message; no user action is required.
SQL Server is starting at normal priority base (=7). This is an informational message only. No user action is required.
Detected 44645 MB of RAM. This is an informational message; no user action is required.

I can see that eight logical processors were detected, which is what I expect given I deployed an 8-vCore instance.

The memory detected is how much memory the SQL Server engine detects from the host or VM. You will see in several places in the book how Azure will use Windows Job Objects to limit the memory visible to SQL Server to enforce resource limits per service tier and vCores. You will find for this Managed Instance the job object will not allow SQL Server to access all the memory as shown here in the ERRORLOG. In fact, you should never rely on what the ERRORLOG shows but instead on the DMV sys.dm_os_job_object, which I will show you how to use in the next section.

Verification Queries

Whenever I install a SQL Server, I typically use a few T-SQL queries as a sanity check. Let us look at these and the results from the Managed Instance compared to SQL Server: 

SELECT @@version
Microsoft SQL Azure (RTM) - 12.0.2000.8   May 28 2024 14:55:16   Copyright (C) 2022 Microsoft Corporation

I explained in Chapter 1 of the book describing the history of Azure SQL why v12 was a monumental moment for the service. Since that time, we have not changed the major version of 12.

Basically, the major version of Azure SQL Managed Instance has no meaning. It does not line up with any major version of SQL Server since Azure SQL Managed Instance is versionless. I will discuss more the concept of versionless in Chapter 5 of the book. Just know that Microsoft strives to keep instances and databases of Azure SQL up to date with all the right changes and fixes, so you do not worry about applying updates.

Note

Even though the version doesn’t change, the timestamp of @@VERSION is when we have last updated the instance. It is not a guarantee, but I’ve never seen this not be the case.

SELECT database_id, name, compatibility_level FROM sys.databases;
database_id    name        compatibility_level
1              master      160
2              tempdb      160
3              model       160
4              msdb        160

This looks normal (I have not created any user databases) except the mssqlsystemresource database is not listed as with SQL Server (it does exist as you can see in the ERRORLOG). Notice the compatibility level is set to 160 which is the dbcompat for SQL Server 2022. 

SELECT name, object_id, type_desc FROM sys.objects;

The results from this query are what you would normally think from a master database on a SQL Server with system tables at the top of the list. 

SELECT * FROM sys.dm_os_schedulers;

Since we have deployed an 8-vCore Managed Instance, I would expect eight VISIBLE ONLINE schedulers, which is the case. I also expect to see a few HIDDEN ONLINE schedulers which are in the result of this query. 

SELECT * FROM sys.dm_os_sys_info;

This is a DMV that provides system information about SQL Server. I can see from the output of this DMV the number of CPUs detected, the amount of memory detected, the target (max server memory), the total memory used by the engine, and the actual worker thread max (1640 for 8 vCores) and that the conventional memory model is used. I will talk more about locked pages in Chapter 5 of the book. 

SELECT * FROM sys.dm_os_process_memory;

This DMV shows OS-related memory information, including whether locked or large pages are used (they are not for Azure SQL Managed Instance or Database). I normally just use this as a sanity check that enough memory is available for SQL Server in a VM or server. 

SELECT * FROM sys.dm_exec_requests;

This is one of the most common DMVs in the world to check the state of what is running on a SQL Server. I run this just to make sure all the normal background processes are running, including LAZY WRITER, RECOVERY WRITER, LOCK MONITOR, etc., and that I can see active queries. 

SELECT SERVERPROPERTY('EngineEdition');

Per the documentation at https://learn.microsoft.com/sql/t-sql/functions/serverproperty-transact-sql, the value of eight is for a Managed Instance.

There are also two new DMVs specific to Azure not found in SQL Server: 

SELECT * FROM sys.dm_user_db_resource_governance;

This DMV is really intended to show you resource limits for a specific Azure SQL Database, but it also works for Managed Instance (you will see a row for all databases, including system databases except for tempdb). You can view limits like memory, max storage, log rates, etc. You can read the documentation for this DMV at https://learn.microsoft.com/sql/relational-databases/system-dynamic-management-views/sys-dm-user-db-resource-governor-azure-sql-database. Note that the docs say this is mostly for internal use, which means it might change in the future.

Note

There is an undocumented DMV called sys.dm_instance_resource_governance which shows resource limits at the instance level.

SELECT * FROM sys.dm_os_job_object;

This is a DMV specific to Azure that shows resource limits Azure applies to a Managed Instance using Windows Job Objects. The specific column I look at is memory_limit_mb which shows me the true amount of memory the Managed Instance has access to. I talked about Windows Job Objects in the section “Implementation Details.”

You may be asking at this point what is so special about Managed Instance since from the perspective of using a tool like SSMS it feels like a SQL Server running in an Azure Virtual Machine. This is the point of a Managed Instance. We want you to have the feel of a SQL Server instance, but not worry about the details you might have to consider with a virtual machine. And since Azure SQL Managed Instance (MI) is a PaaS service, you will see the benefits of using MI especially when it comes to a versionless SQL Server, predictable performance, built-in high availability, and disaster recovery.

Migrating to Azure SQL Managed Instance

I discussed earlier in this chapter some of the considerations and tools for migrating to Azure SQL Managed Instance.

Let me add more details on some considerations should you consider when migrating:

  • When you are migrating a database protected by Transparent Data Encryption to a managed instance using native restore option, the corresponding certificate from the on-premises or Azure VM SQL Server needs to be migrated before database restore.

  • Restore of system databases is not supported. To migrate instance-level objects (stored in master or msdb databases), we recommend scripting them out and run T-SQL scripts on the destination instance. For example, you can use SSMS to script out SQL Server Agent jobs to T-SQL.

  • Database compatibility is fully supported by Azure SQL Managed Instance, including all current supported versions of SQL Server. I have many customers that choose to keep their current dbcompat when migrating to Azure and then test and update it later. Read more about dbcompat at https://aka.ms/dbcompat.

  • While you can always restore a full backup (offline) to Azure SQL Managed Instance, you have online choices as well, including a log-shipping approach with Azure Database Migration Service (DMS) and a more granular approach using Managed Instance Link using Always On Availability Groups. Managed Instance link works even if you don’t have an AG deployed on-premises. Learn more about how to use Managed Instance link at https://aka.ms/milink.

  • Application changes should be minimal for a migration to Managed Instance except using the new server name (which you saw in the previous part of this chapter on how to connect) and possibly the authentication method. A new capability to use Windows Authentication and mitigate changes you need in your application for authentication is now available. Learn more at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/managed-instance/winauth-azuread-setup.

Deploying an Azure SQL Database

Deploying an Azure SQL Database is both different and like Azure SQL Managed Instance. The experience is similar as you will use the Azure Portal or CLI (or even T-SQL) to deploy a database but different since you are…well deploying a database, not an instance of SQL Server. Since you are deploying just a database, you get more options as I have described in this book, including Serverless, elastic pools, and Hyperscale.

The basic process to deploy Azure SQL Database is

  • ✓ Decide to deploy a single database or elastic pool

  • ✓ Choose a resource group and region

  • ✓ Choose an existing or new logical database server

  • ✓ Choose your purchasing model, compute model, service tier, hardware configuration, and sizes

  • ✓ Optionally supply other configuration choices

  • ✓ Deploy it!

Deployment and Options

To get started, I will use the Azure SQL screen to deploy by searching for Azure SQL in the marketplace as I showed you in Chapter 3 with SQL Server on Azure Virtual Machine but the single database on the left-hand side and click Create. I will show you how to deploy a single database which will allow me to also deploy a database server (and I will describe what and why you need a database server).

As we did for Azure SQL Managed Instance, let’s walk through all the options to deploy the database through the Azure Portal.

Basics

If you choose a single database, you are presented with a Basics screen like Azure SQL Managed Instance as seen in Figure 4-16.

Figure 4-16

Basics screen to deploy an Azure SQL Database

You can see from my screen that I have already created a new resource group, defined a database name, and have the option to first create a new database (or logical) server.

Note

Notice the option at the top of this screen to choose the free database offer at https://aka.ms/freedboffer.

You may be wondering why you need a database server when the promise of Azure SQL Database is “you own the database; Azure will manage everything else.”

A database server, also known as a logical server, is a collection of metadata stored in the Azure infrastructure used to organize one more Azure SQL Databases. It is not a single SQL Server instance on a physical server. A database server contains a logical master database just like a true SQL Server instance. Notice the region is associated with the logical server, not the database. Any database created for the logical server will be hosted in the region of the server. All connectivity and networking will be associated with the logical server. In fact, you could create a logical server first, connect to that server, and use T-SQL CREATE DATABASE to create Azure SQL Databases. The login and password you supply for the logical server becomes a login in the logical master database, which is a server-level principal who is effectively a server admin for all databases.

You can see my choices in Figure 4-17 to create the logical server, including your region and preferred authentication method. In this case, I’m going to set up both Microsoft Entra and SQL Authentication and supply an admin for each.

Figure 4-17

Creating a logical server for Azure SQL Database

Once you click OK for the logical server (it will be created as part of the deployment), you can choose if you want to make this part of an elastic pool, but I won’t do that here. You can create a logical server on its own first and then create databases for it. In this scenario, I’m creating a new database along with a logical server and have the database placed with that new server.

Now I have more choices to complete the Basics screen as seen in Figure 4-18.

Figure 4-18

Completing Basics screen choices for Azure SQL Database

The Workload environment is a convenient method to choose a lower service tier and vCore size for Development or choose the Production option which will default to Hyperscale. I’ll choose Production.

You then have a choice to configure several aspects of your deployment, including service tier and other resource choices. You can select Configure database to make these choices. Before I show you those options, you also have a choice on backup storage redundancy options. This is like the choice for Azure SQL Managed Instance. Unless you have data residency requirements, I recommend you pick a geo-redundant option. Geo-zone redundancy has the highest level of redundancy and cost, but a zone redundant option is required if you choose zone redundancy as a deployment option. In fact, I’ll choose a zone redundant option for the deployment, and this option will change for my backup option.

Figure 4-19 shows the options when you select Configure database.

Figure 4-19

Compute and storage options for Azure SQL Database

Let’s go through each of these options.

Service Tier

I mentioned earlier your choices of General Purpose (GP), Business Critical (BC), and Hyperscale.

Compute Tier

For GP and Hyperscale, you can choose Serverless or Provisioned. I’ll show you more about Serverless later in the chapter.

Hardware Configuration

This is where you can choose Gen5, FSv2, DC, or Premium Hardware choices.

vCores

Based on your service tier and hardware configuration, you can choose how many vCores you need (which you can scale up or down later). You can have as many as 128 vCores.

HA Replicas and Zone Redundancy

Hyperscale allows up to 30 named replicas, so you can choose your number of replicas here. I’ll cover more about Hyperscale Availability in Chapter 8 of the book. You don’t have to have any replicas for Hyperscale, but if you choose zone redundancy, you need to have at least one replica.

Other Choices

  • If you have selected GP or BC tiers, you need to choose a max storage size. You can have up to 4TB for GP and for BC. For Hyperscale, there is no maximum storage, so you don’t need to make a choice.

  • For GP and BC service tiers, you can choose Azure Hybrid Benefit for licensing. Hyperscale’s new pricing structure doesn’t need this choice.

I realize there are so many choices here; it is hard to keep up. I created this slide for a presentation I did that you might find useful as seen in Figure 4-20.

Figure 4-20

Azure SQL Database deployment choices

Networking

Click Next: Networking > to see your choices for connectivity and network security as seen in Figure 4-21.

Figure 4-21

Networking choices when deploying an Azure SQL Database

Unlike Azure SQL Managed Instance, Azure SQL Database is not part of a private virtual network. You have three choices during deployment:

  • No access – Deploy the database but do not allow any connectivity until you are ready to make your choice.

  • Public endpoint – Expose connectivity of the logical server and/or database to the public within Azure or to the Internet (or both).

  • Private endpoint – This is a new addition to Azure SQL Database to make it very secure. This allows you to deny public access to your server and/or database and only allow private connectivity within defined virtual networks in and outside of Azure.

For now, I will select the public endpoint and set Allow Azure services and resources access this server to Yes. This allows me to deploy an Azure Virtual Machine (no matter what virtual network) and connect to this database or to connect with SQL client tools on the client computer where I am currently deploying the browser. I will show you how to tighten up the security of this model in Chapter 6 of the book.

I also have choices here like an Azure SQL Managed Instance for connection policy (Proxy or Redirect) and a minimum TLS version.

Security

Click Next: Security > to set several security-related options as you can see in Figure 4-22.

Figure 4-22

Security choices for Azure SQL Database

Like Azure SQL Managed Instance, you can enable Microsoft Defender for SQL and get capabilities like Vulnerability Assessments and Advanced Thread Protection.

Ledger is a feature that provides tamper-evident record of changes in the database, like a blockchain inside SQL. You will learn more about Ledger in Chapter 10 of the book.

Server identity allows you to configure a managed identity for passwordless applications using Microsoft Entra.

Transparent Data Encryption (TDE), encryption at rest, is on by default for Azure SQL Database. This option gives you the ability to Bring Your Own Key (BYOK).

Your last option is to configure Secure Enclaves for Always Encrypted. Always Encrypted is an end-to-end encryption capability for SQL. Learn more about Secure Enclaves at https://learn.microsoft.com/sql/relational-databases/security/encryption/always-encrypted-enclaves.

Additional Settings

Click Next: Additional settings > to see more options for the deployment. Figure 4-23 shows these additional options.

Figure 4-23

Additional settings for the deployment of Azure SQL Database

Your first choice is to either create a blank database or create a database based on a backup of a geo-replicated Azure SQL Database or from the sample AdventureWorksLT (LT stands for light). You can learn more about how to restore from a geo-replicated backup at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/recovery-using-backups.

Your next choice is database collation. Since I chose a sample database, the collation is already decided. For a new blank database, it is important to choose this during deployment because you cannot change it later.

Tags

Click Next: Tags > to define an optional tag for the deployment.

Deploy It!

Click Next: Review + create > to see the final validation screen as you can see from any Azure resource deployment. Just like with Managed Instance, you can see estimated costs, terms of use, privacy policy, a review of your choices, and the ability to download an Azure template.

Click Create to see the deployment take off. Just like with Managed Instance if you do not leave this screen, you will see the progress of your deployment in both the notification area of the portal and on your main screen.

In my example, the deployment only took a matter of minutes as shown in Figure 4-24.

Figure 4-24

A completed deployment of Azure SQL Database

Just like a Managed Instance deployment, you can click More events in the Activity Log to see the sequence of deploying all resources.

Click Go to resource to see the Overview screen on the database like Figure 4-25.

Figure 4-25

The Overview screen of an Azure SQL Database

Just like with a virtual machine and Managed Instance, the portal shows a Service Menu, Command Bar, Working Pane, and Monitoring Pane. While this looks like other Azure resources, most of this information is specific to Azure SQL Database. We will use many of these options throughout the rest of the book as you explore security, performance, availability, and other features. I’ll discuss some specific configuration choices for the database in Chapter 5 of the book.

Deploying Serverless

Let’s first look at how to deploy a Serverless database for Hyperscale by looking at the unique difference on specifying vCores.

On the Overview screen of the database you just deployed, click the server name. This brings you to a screen like Figure 4-26.

Figure 4-26

Overview of a logical server for Azure SQL Database

Click + Create Database in the Command Menu to create a new database for this logical server.

On the Basics screen for this new database, I’ll use a name of bwsqldbserverless and then select Configure database. I’ll then use choices like Figure 4-27 to choose a Serverless deployment.

Figure 4-27

Deploying a serverless Hyperscale database

Notice here with Serverless I can now choose a min and max vCore range. My database can scale up and down automatically based on my resource needs. And the key factor for Serverless is that I only pay for the vCores I use per second with the min vCore as the min fixed cost.

All my other options are the same as my first database deployment. Just like I can change the number of fixed vCores for a Provisioned database, I can change the min and max for serverless later. I’ll discuss later about configuring a Serverless database in Chapter 5 of the book.

Note

A General Purpose service tier serverless database also has an option for auto-pause, but that is not available at the time of the writing of this book for Hyperscale.

Deploying with a CLI

An Azure SQL Database can be deployed with command-line interfaces (CLI) through the az sql db (https://learn.microsoft.com/cli/azure/sql/db) command interfaces or through New-AzSQLDatabase PowerShell cmdlet (https://learn.microsoft.com/powershell/module/az.sql/new-azsqldatabase).

Unlike Managed Instance, it is easier to use the az CLI for Azure SQL Database without an Azure template because I only have to create the logical server and then I can create the database (I don’t have to create a virtual network and all the components first).

Tip

It is easy to create a database with a very small az CLI command like the following command:

az sql db create -n sqldbiseasy -g bwsqldbrg -s bwsqllogicalserver

and within a few minutes I have deployed a General Purpose 2 vCore database. This assumes I’ve already created the resource group and logical server, but it shows how simple it can be to create a new database.

You can also deploy Azure SQL Database using Bicep (https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/single-database-create-bicep-quickstart) and Terraform (https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/single-database-create-terraform-quickstart).

In addition, as I showed earlier in this chapter, you can use Copilot in Azure to get easy install steps to deploy an Azure SQL Database using the az CLI.

Implementation Details

Note

These implementation details may change over time as we change and improve the service. I offer up some of these details, so you can understand how we build, manage, and run the service.

In Chapter 1 of this book, I covered the incredible history of how we have built architecture to power millions of databases for Azure SQL Database. Let me give you a few more details about how we implement Azure SQL Database behind the scenes.

Note

As I interviewed many people in the Microsoft engineering team behind the scenes about Azure SQL Database even as I write this chapter, we are looking into how to make our implementation more efficient. Therefore, it is possible even some of these details could be a bit outdated by the time you read this chapter. That is the speed of the cloud and an author’s nightmare!

Dedicated Rings and Instances

Unlike Managed Instance, we pre-deploy rings dedicated to hosting Azure SQL Databases. With only a few exceptions, each database is hosted by a dedicated SQL Server instance (exceptions being “subcore” DTU options and elastic pools). This allows us to provide better isolation for a customer and keep the “just worry about the database” model while opening some instance-level surface area (e.g., DMVs and columnstore indexes). We may provision these instances on the same VM or node, but those details are abstracted from you provided we keep to our Service Level Agreement (SLA) and objectives for performance.

All the rings and instances are powered and managed using Azure Service Fabric. This is the same fabric software that you can build your own microservices. The Azure Service Fabric architecture is well documented at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/service-fabric/service-fabric-architecture.

The Logical Server

As I stated earlier in this chapter, a database or logical server is just a metadata concept. We provide an interface to a server and a master database. But, when you query various aspects of the master database, we may be pulling data from other stores or files within the service to show you the information. I remember getting this question from Anna Hoffman as we built out our now famous Azure SQL for Beginners series (https://aka.ms/azuresql4beginners). What I told Anna is that with SQL Server a master database has its own metadata and the physical location of the other databases on the server, which is why you can “switch” database context very easily (i.e., USE <database>). In this case of Azure SQL Database and a logical server, the master contains metadata stored on gateway servers and locations of nodes where user databases exist for the server. It is kind of difficult to switch context to another server easily with a USE statement.

Storage, Compute, and Gateways

You will see in Chapter 8 for availability more details on the architecture behind the scenes on how we implement high availability (HA) for General Purpose, Business Critical, and Hyperscale service tiers. We achieve certain HA capabilities by either using Azure Storage or local storage with technologies like Always On Availability Groups.

In each of these cases, one of the key components for connectivity is a gateway. Gateways are nodes that basically route traffic to nodes hosting SQL Server databases. I mentioned the use of redirect vs. proxy connection types with a Managed Instance earlier in the chapter. The same concept will apply with an Azure SQL Database. Gateways are critical to connectivity in that they provide abstractions to an application for connectivity no matter where the node for the database lives, which you will learn more about in Chapter 6 on security and Chapter 8 on availability.

Serverless

Serverless compute involves several interesting technologies we implement within the standard deployment of a SQL Server. Many of these details are described in our documentation at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/serverless-tier-overview.

Since storage and compute are separated for a Serverless deployment, pausing a database is not that difficult since no application is connected. We just need to keep around enough state information that when a new login is made, we can connect the database to an instance and “warm up” the application.

Autoscaling is more interesting. We need to scale up or down the database CPU resources without application disruption. And if we can scale on a node that can meet the new scaling demand, there is no disruption. However, if we cannot meet that demand, we may need to use an Azure Load Balancer to keep the application connected if possible until a new node is found to meet demand, but there can be a disconnection when the new node is brought up.

Memory management is also different in that we must deploy memory policies to reclaim memory for the SQL Server instance when CPU or cache utilization is low. Think of this concept as though we are signaling SQL Server there is external memory pressure and lowering the target for memory. Autoscaling and memory management for Serverless is described more at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/serverless-tier-overview?view=azuresql&tabs=general-purpose#autoscaling.

Hyperscale

Hyperscale is a unique implementation for a database within the same architecture of databases (dedicated rings and nodes) implemented much differently than General Purpose or Business Critical service tiers.

A Hyperscale deployment involves a series of nodes for compute, logging, and caching combined with Azure Storage. I talked about this architecture in Chapter 1 of the book, but it is worth showing you again as seen in Figure 4-31 (which is directly from the documentation at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/service-tier-hyperscale?view=azuresql#distributed-functions-architecture).

Figure 4-31

The Hyperscale architecture

I will show you more of the working parts of this architecture in Chapter 8 of this book, but let me stop and call out a few key components:

  • Separate compute and storage

Just like General Purpose, we store database files on Azure Storage. But notice here we use Azure Standard Storage. Speed to access the database files is not as important because of the caching system.

  • The caching system

We use a combination of page servers (actual nodes that host database pages) and buffer pool caches (think SSD drives that extend the buffer pool) on page servers and compute nodes.

  • Log Service

For Hyperscale, any logged changes are still in a log cache on the primary compute node. However, when log changes must be flushed to disk (a commit), these I/O requests are redirected to another node where a component called the Log Service runs (this is also called Xlog). The Log Service is responsible for ensuring changes are stored locally (called the landing zone) and are refreshed to the caching system, secondary replicas, and eventually to Azure Storage.

  • Decoupled replicas

In a way, Hyperscale provides you with the best of both the General Purpose and Business Critical tiers. The actual database and transaction log files are stored in Azure Storage (which has its own redundancy), but we also have replicas for extremely fast high availability.

However, the secondary replica system does not use Always On Availability Groups. In fact, the primary and secondary replicas are not aware of each other. Secondary replicas use a log change methodology but are fed changes from the Log Service. Commits on the primary replica can proceed once the Log Service has hardened the changes.

High availability is even allowed with no secondary replicas. How? If the primary node has an issue, we can deploy a new primary replica on a new node and use page servers or even the database files on Azure Storage because it is decoupled. Having said this, RTO is much faster with the presence of secondary replicas. The secondary replica system (because you can have four of these) provides the best read-scale option for Azure SQL Database.

  • Hyperfast backup and restore

Because most of the data access comes from the caching system, reading database pages from the database files is rare with a warm system. This allows us to use snapshot backups for database files. Snapshot backups are extremely fast since we just copy the files to another storage location. And the other amazing story is restore. Restoring a database snapshot is crazy fast!

Resource Governance

To meet the SLA requirements for Azure SQL Database, we must put some resource limits on the usage of the database. I have described some of these limits in this chapter. Behind the scenes, we use these technologies to enforce these limits.

SQL Server Resource Governor

Azure SQL Managed Instance allows user defined workload groups and pools. Azure SQL Database uses Resource Governor behind the scenes to enforce certain limits. Moving to a dedicated SQL Server instance was a key driver in allowing us to use Resource Governor.

Engine enhancements

The engine has been enhanced in Azure to detect the generation of a certain size and rate of transaction log records and govern the application if necessary. The primary signal this governance is happening is seeing a wait type of LOG_RATE_GOVERNOR. You can read more about log rate governance at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/resource-limits-logical-server?view=azuresql#transaction-log-rate-governance.

Windows Job Objects

I have mentioned this technology before in this chapter. Windows Job Objects allow us to control resource usage on the SQL Server engine process to ensure we properly enforce resource limits like memory.

File Source Resource Manager (FSRM)

FSRM provides a mechanism, so we can properly enforce storage maximum sizes outside of what we control through SQL Server file size limits.

We have created a nice blog post talking about how we enforce resource limits using these technologies and why we use them at https://azure.microsoft.com/blog/resource-governance-in-azure-sql-database/.

Connecting and Verifying Deployment

Once you deploy an Azure SQL Database, you will likely want to quickly connect and verify aspects of the deployment.

Connecting to Azure SQL Database

Earlier in this chapter, I deployed my logical server and Azure SQL Database, allowing public endpoint access with options to access through Azure services and with a firewall rule for the client IP address where I deployed through the portal. I will show you how to connect using both techniques.

Because I used the option Allow Azure services and resources to access this server when I deployed the logical server for the database, I can use an Azure Virtual Machine or even sqlcmd from the Azure Cloud Shell to connect to this server and database.

To connect with Azure Cloud Shell, you need to find the name of the logical server for the deployment. There are many ways to do this through the portal. You can simply look at your resource groups or resources from the home of the portal and find the server bwsqllogicalserver (or your name).

Figure 4-32 shows the Working Pane of the server with the server name to use when connecting with a SQL Server tool or application.

Figure 4-32

Find the server name to connect

Notice I clicked next to the server name to copy the Fully Qualified Domain Name (FQDN) to the clipboard. You will find the FQDN is a combination of the logical server name and .database.windows.net.

Note

Azure SQL Database also supports the concept of a DNS alias, which you can read about at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/dns-alias-overview.

I can now bring up the Azure Cloud Shell (you can use the home page of the portal or https://shell.azure.com).

Since sqlcmd is installed in the cloud shell, I can use a syntax like Figure 4-33.

Figure 4-33

Using sqlcmd from the Azure Cloud Shell

I could also connect inside a VM in Azure with a tool like SSMS, but let me show you another way.

I also configured the logical server for a firewall rule for the IP address of the computer when I was using the Azure Portal. In the Working Pane of the logical server, I can select Show firewall settings to see the following information. Figure 4-34 shows this firewall setting along with other network options.

Figure 4-34

Configure firewall rules for Azure SQL Database

A firewall rule is very much like a firewall rule you would configure in Windows Server or Linux. If you have used SQL Server before you know that by default, we do not open the firewall rules in the OS for port 1433 by default. The firewall rule above is opening access to the gateway for this logical server for this specific IP address. Firewall rules can be specified at the logical server or database level. You can read more about firewall rules for Azure SQL Database at https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/azure-sql/database/firewall-configure. I will talk more about using a more secure method to connect to Azure SQL Database called Private Link in Chapter 6 of the book.

Since my client IP address is in the firewall rule, I can use a tool like SQL Server Management Studio to connect to the logical server from my client laptop as seen in Figure 4-35.

Figure 4-35

Connecting to Azure SQL Database with SSMS

You can see here I used Microsoft Entra authentication since I added my Microsoft Entra account as an admin when I deployed the logical server.
